The Three Types of Objections in Munazarah
Author classifies objections based on whether the premise, evidence, or conclusion is denied.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between naqd, munaqada, and mu'arada based on the target of the objection and the presence of proof.

Core Idea
The three objections differ by what is denied and whether proof is given — naqd is bare denial, munaqada is denial with proof, mu'arada counters the evidence.

In rigorous debate (munazarah), not all objections are created equal. Classical logicians classified objections into three types based on what they attack and how. This passage defines the core terms you need to navigate any dialectical exchange with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the objection
Determine whether the objection attacks the premise (muqaddima), the evidence (dalil), or the conclusion (madlul). This is the first dimension of classification.
The passage starts with 'فإن منع المقدمة أو الدليل أو المدلول'.
2
Check for the presence of proof
If the target is a premise, determine whether the objection provides a proof (shahid) or is a bare denial. If it attacks the evidence/conclusion, it is automatically mu'arada.
The author distinguishes 'بغير شاهد' and 'بشاهد' for the premise.
3
Apply the correct term
Match the combination: premise denied without proof = naqd; premise denied with proof = munaqada; evidence or conclusion denied with counter-evidence = mu'arada.
Memorize: naqd = bare denial, munaqada = denial with proof, mu'arada = counter-evidence.
4
Practise with a sample objection
Given an objection like 'Your premise is false because reason shows otherwise', classify it. Then create your own examples from theological debates.
Use the three categories to map real objections from kalam discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن منع المقدمة أو الدليل أو المدلول فالأول [؟] إن منع بغير شاهد فهو النقض، والثاني إن منع بشاهد فهو المناقضة، والثالث إن منع بالدليل فهو المعارضة.
If [the opponent] denies the premise, the evidence, or the conclusion: the first, if denied without proof, is naqd; the second, if denied with proof, is munaqada; the third, if denied with evidence [counter-evidence], is mu'arada.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage outlines the three primary forms of objection in a debate. The author considers three possible targets: the premise (muqaddima), the evidence (dalil), or the conclusion (madlul). For each, the nature of the objection differs. Denying a premise without providing proof is called naqd (refutation by counterexample or simple denial). Denying a premise with proof is called munaqada (contradiction with evidence). Denying the conclusion or evidence by providing an alternative argument is called mu'arada (counter-argument). The text emphasizes that mere denial without proof is invalid in rigorous debate.

Key Terms
النقض
المناقضة
المعارضة
المقدمة
الدليل
المدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the term for denying a premise without providing proof?
A) Munaqada
B) Naqd
C) Mu'arada
D) I'tirad

Key Takeaway
The three types of objections are distinguished by their target and the presence of proof, forming the basic vocabulary of dialectical critique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the objection: 'The premise that Allah has a body is false because the Quran says "Nothing is like Him."' Identify the type of objection and explain why.
?
Construct a brief debate exchange where one party uses munaqada against a premise in a theological argument about divine attribes.

The Three Valid Objections in Dialectic: Munaqadah, Naqd, Mu'aradah
Defines munazarah and the three valid objection types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between munaqadah, naqd, and mu'aradah by identifying what each objects to (premise, proof, conclusion) and whether evidence is required.

Core Idea
Not every objection in debate is valid—the questioner must target the right component with the right proof.

This passage from the Risalah al-Adab establishes the rules for a proper dialectical exchange. The questioner (sa'il) has only three legitimate types of objection, each corresponding to a denial of a specific part of the argument. Understanding these distinctions prevents chaotic debate and ensures disagreements remain substantive and orderly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of denial
Determine whether the questioner is denying a premise of the proof, the proof itself, or the conclusion. This is the first key distinction.
2
Check if evidence is provided
For each denial, note whether the questioner brings any evidence or counter-proof. The validity of the objection depends on this.
3
Map to the correct term
If a premise is denied without proof (or with an attribution but no proof), it is munaqadah. If the proof itself is denied with evidence, it is naqd. If the conclusion is denied with a counter-proof, it is mu'aradah.
Denial without any support is always invalid (mukabarah).
4
Recognize what is rejected
Denial of a premise with a proof is initially rejected, but allowed after first establishing the premise's denial. Denial of the proof without evidence is universally rejected. Denial of the conclusion without proof is also rejected.
The text specifies these as the only valid paths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن المناظرة هي النظر بالبصيرة من الجانبين في النسبة بين الشيئين إظهار الصواب. ولكل من الجانبين وظائف وللمناظرة آداب. أما وظيفة السائل فثلثة: المناقضة والنقض والمعارضة. لأنه إنما يمنع مقدمة الدليل أو الدليل نفسه أو المدلول. فإن كان الأول فان منع مجرداً أو بمسند فهو المناقضة. ومنها نوع يُسمى بالحل وهو تعيين موضع الغلط. وأما منعه بالدليل فهو غضب غير مسموع عند المحققين. نعم قد يتوجه ذلك بعد إقامة الدليل على تلك المقدمة الممنوعة. وإن كان الثاني فان منع بشاهد فهو النقض. وأما منعه بلا شاهد فهو مكابرة غير مسموعة اتفاقاً. وإن كان الثالث فان منع بالدليل فهو المعارضة. وما بين التربية منها بهم نال فهو مكابرة غير مسموع عنه اتفاقاً.
Know that munazarah (dialectic) is the examination by insight from both sides of the relation between two things for the manifestation of correctness. And for each of the two sides there are functions, and for munazarah there are etiquettes. As for the function of the questioner, it is three: munaqadah (denial), naqd (refutation), and mu'aradah (opposition). For he only denies either a premise of the proof, or the proof itself, or the conclusion. If it is the first, then if he denies it absolutely or with an attribution, it is munaqadah. And among it is a type called al-hall (solution), which is identifying the place of error. As for denying it with a proof, it is ghathab (illegitimate anger) not entertained by the verifiers. Yes, it may be directed after establishing the proof for that denied premise. If it is the second, then if he denies it with evidence, it is naqd. As for denying it without evidence, it is obstinacy not entertained unanimously. If it is the third, then if he denies it with a proof, it is mu'aradah. And what is between [the types] from al-terbiyah? with them he gained? is obstinacy not entertained by him unanimously.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage defines munazarah (dialectic) as a mutual examination between two parties regarding a relation between two things, aiming to reveal the truth. It then focuses on the role of the questioner (sa'il) in a debate, specifying three valid functions: munaqadah, naqd, and mu'aradah. The distinction is based on what the questioner denies: a premise of the proof, the proof itself, or the conclusion. If he denies a premise without a proof or with an attribution (without an independent proof), it is munaqadah. If he identifies the exact mistake, it is called al-hall. If he brings a proof against the premise, it is rejected as ghathab (illegitimate anger), unless he first establishes the proof and then denies. If he denies the proof itself with evidence, it is naqd; without evidence, it is sheer obstinacy. If he denies the conclusion with a proof, it is mu'aradah. Any other objection is considered obstinacy and not accepted by scholars of dialectic. This passage guards against improper debate tactics by clarifying the permitted forms of challenge.

Key Terms
المناظرة
المناقضة
النقض
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Risalah, what is the correct action when a questioner denies a premise without providing any evidence or attribution?
A) It is a valid munaqadah.
B) It is an invalid munaqadah and should be accepted.
C) It is a form of mu'aradah.
D) It is rejected as obstinacy (mukabarah).

Key Takeaway
The questioner in a dialectical debate has only three legitimate moves: denying a premise (munaqadah), refuting the proof (naqd), or opposing the conclusion with a proof (mu'aradah); any other objection is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify each of the following objections in a debate as munaqadah, naqd, mu'aradah, or invalid: (a) 'I deny that everything temporally originated has a cause without giving proof.' (b) 'Your proof is invalid because it commits the fallacy of begging the question.' (c) 'I accept your premises but I have a proof that the world is eternal.' (d) 'You are wrong and I refuse to listen.'
?
Construct a valid mu'aradah objection against the claim: 'Alcohol is forbidden because it is an intoxicant.' (Hint: Provide a counter-proof against the conclusion that alcohol is forbidden, perhaps showing that it is not always forbidden or that the 'illah is not present.)

Definition of Munazarah and Three Functions of the Questioner
Author defines munazarah and explains the three functions of the questioner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of munazarah and distinguish the three functions of the questioner (sa'il) with their conditions.

Core Idea
Munazarah is mutual scrutiny for truth; the questioner attacks either premise, proof, or conclusion.

This passage from a classical debate manual gives you the foundational definition of munazarah (disputation) and then breaks down the three legitimate ways the questioner can challenge an opponent's argument. Understanding these moves is essential to mastering the art of Islamic dialectic (jadal). Let's walk through the definition and then the three attack types.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of munazarah
Read the Arabic: 'اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُنَاظَرَةَ: هِيَ النَّظَرُ بِالْبَصِيرَةِ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فِي النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْتَيْنِ؛ إِظْهَارًا لِلصَّوَابِ.' Identify the genus (intellectual scrutiny) and differentia (from both sides, concerning relationship, for truth).
2
Identify the three possible targets of attack
The questioner can attack either the premise of the proof, the proof itself, or the conclusion. These correspond to the three functions: munaqada, naqd, and mu'arada.
The passage lists them in order: 'فَإِمَّا أَنْ يَمْنَعَ مُقَدِّمَةَ الدَّلِيلِ، أَوِ الدَّلِيلَ نَفْسَهُ، أَوِ الْمَدْلُولَ.'
3
Learn the two types of munaqada and al-haqq
Munaqada is denying the premise. It can be mujarrad (without citation) or bi'l-sanad (with citation). A subtype is al-haqq, which is specifying the exact error. Denying the premise with a proof (ghasb) is not accepted unless the premise has already been proven.
The author considers ghasb as causing confusion (khabt).
4
Learn the condition for naqd
Naqd is denying the proof itself by bringing a counterexample (shahid). Without a shahid, it is obstinacy (mukabara) and unanimously rejected.
The passage says: 'وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي: فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالشَّاهِدِ فَهُوَ النَّقْضُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا شَاهِدٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ اتِّفَاقًا.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُنَاظَرَةَ: هِيَ النَّظَرُ بِالْبَصِيرَةِ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فِي النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْتَيْنِ؛ إِظْهَارًا لِلصَّوَابِ.
وَلِكُلٍّ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ وَظَائِفُ، وَلِلْمُنَاظَرَةِ آدَابٌ . أَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ السَّائِلِ فَثَلَاثَةٌ : الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَالنَّقْضُ، وَالْمُعَارَضَةُ؛ لأَنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يَمْنَعَ مُقَدِّمَةَ الدَّلِيلِ ، أَوِ الدَّلِيلَ نَفْسَهُ، أَوِ الْمَدْلُولَ.
فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ مُجَرَّدًا ، أَوْ بِالسَّنَدِ فَهُوَ الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَمِنْهَا نَوْعٌ يُسَمَّى بِالْحَقِّ، وَهُوَ تَعْيِينُ مَوْضِعِ الغَلَطِ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ غَصْبٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعِ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ؛ لاسْتِلْزَامِهِ الْخَبْط ، نَعَمْ قَدْ يَتَوَجَّهُ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ .
وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي: فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالشَّاهِدِ فَهُوَ النَّقْضُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا شَاهِدٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ اتِّفَاقًا.
Know that munazarah (disputation) is: intellectual scrutiny from both sides concerning the relationship between two things, for the manifestation of truth. Each of the two sides has functions, and munazarah has etiquettes. As for the function of the questioner (sa'il), there are three: munaqadah (opposing the premise), naqd (counterexample/objection with evidence), and mu'aradah (counter-argument/opposing the conclusion). For he either prevents the premise of the proof, or the proof itself, or the conclusion. If it is the first: if he prevents it without citation or with citation (sourced), it is munaqadah. A type of it is called 'al-haqq' (the right/correct), which is determining the place of error. As for his prevention with a proof (argument), it is usurpation (ghasb) not listened to according to the muhaqqiqun (verifiers), because it necessitates confusion. Yes, that may be valid after establishing a proof for that prevented premise. And if it is the second: if he prevents with a witness/counterexample, it is naqd. As for his prevention without a witness, it is obstinacy (mukabara) not listened to unanimously.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines munazarah and outlines the duties of the questioner (sa'il) in a debate. Munazarah is defined as mutual intellectual scrutiny by both parties regarding the relationship between two things (the thesis and its opposite or two propositions), with the aim of revealing truth. Each side has functions; the author focuses on the sa'il's three functions, which correspond to three possible attacks: munaqadah (challenging the premise), naqd (challenging the proof itself with a counterexample), and mu'aradah (challenging the conclusion). These arise because the sa'il can either deny the premise of the opponent's proof, deny the proof itself, or deny the conclusion. The author then elaborates on the first two. For munaqadah: if the sa'il simply denies the premise without proof, or with a citation, it is munaqadah. A subtype is 'al-haqq' which is pointing out the exact error. However, if the sa'il denies the premise using a proof (i.e., he provides a counter-argument), this is considered ghasb (usurpation) and is not accepted by the verifiers, because it leads to confusion in the debate. It can be allowed only after establishing proof for the denied premise. For naqd: if the sa'il denies the proof with a witness (a counterexample), that is naqd. Denying without a witness is obstinacy and unanimously rejected. The third function (mu'aradah) is named but not explained here.

Key Terms
المناظرة
السائل
المناقضة
النقض
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many functions does the sa'il (questioner) have according to this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The questioner in munazarah has three legitimate ways to attack: deny the premise (munaqada), attack the proof with a counterexample (naqd), or oppose the conclusion (mu'arada); each has specific conditions for acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about whether wine is forbidden by consensus, the opponent claims that the prohibition of wine is proven by the Qur'an. The questioner says: 'The verse you cited is abrogated.' Which function of the questioner is this? Is it valid according to the rules? Justify your answer.
?
Create a short debate scenario where the questioner uses naqd (challenge with a counterexample) against a proof. Explain why this is acceptable rather than obstinacy.

The Definition of Munazara
Author defines munazara with its essential components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of munazara and identify its key components (nazar, basira, janibayn, nisba, shay'ayn, izhar sawab).

Core Idea
Munazara is mutual examination by insight to manifest truth.

In this passage, the author defines munazara (scholarly disputation) as a specific kind of examination. Understanding this definition is crucial because it sets the framework for the entire discipline of adab al-bahth. Let's break it down step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus (jins)
The author says munazara is a type of 'nazar' (examination). This is the broader category. What distinguishes it from other types of nazar?
2
Examine the differentia (fasl)
The differentia includes 'bil-basira' (by insight) and 'min al-janibayn' (from both sides). These specify the method and participants.
3
Understand the purpose
The phrase 'izharan li-s-sawab' (to manifest what is correct) indicates the goal. How does this differ from mere debate?
4
Consider the implications
Since it is mutual examination, both parties are seeking truth, not just winning. This is a key principle in Islamic scholarly debate.
The definition emphasizes that munazara is not argumentation for its own sake, but a truth-seeking process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُنَاظَرَةَ: هِيَ النَّظَرُ بِالْبَصِيرَةِ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فِي النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْتَيْنِ؛ إِظْهَارًا لِلصَّوَابِ. وَلِكُلٍّ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ وَظَائِفُ، وَلِلْمُنَاظَرَةِ آدَابٌ . أَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ السَّائِلِ فَثَلَاثَةٌ : الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَالنَّقْضُ، وَالْمُعَارَضَةُ؛ لأَنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يَمْنَعَ مُقَدِّمَةَ الدَّلِيلِ ، أَوِ الدَّلِيلَ نَفْسَهُ، أَوِ الْمَدْلُولَ. فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ مُجَرَّدًا ، أَوْ بِالسَّنَدِ فَهُوَ الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَمِنْهَا نَوْعٌ يُسَمَّى بِالْحَقِّ، وَهُوَ تَعْيِينُ مَوْضِعِ الغَلَطِ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ غَصْبٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعِ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ؛ لاسْتِلْزَامِهِ الْخَبْط ، نَعَمْ قَدْ يَتَوَجَّهُ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ . وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي: فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالشَّاهِدِ فَهُوَ النَّقْضُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا شَاهِدٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ اتِّفَاقًا. وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّالِثُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ الْمُعَارَضَةُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا دَلِيلٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةِ أَيْضًا اتفاقاً .
Know that al-munazarah (scholarly disputation) is: the examination by insight from both sides concerning the relation between two things, for the purpose of manifesting what is correct. For each of the two sides there are functions, and for the disputation there are etiquettes. As for the function of the questioner (al-sa'il), they are three: al-munaqadah (contradiction), al-naqd (refutation by counterexample), and al-mu'aradah (counter-argument); because he either denies a premise of the proof, or the proof itself, or the conclusion. If it is the first: then if he denies it without proof or with a precedent, it is al-munaqadah, and from it there is a type called al-haqq (the truth), which is the determination of the place of error. As for his denial with a proof, it is usurpation (ghasb) not accepted by the verifiers, because it necessitates confusion; however, it may be directed after establishing a proof for that denied premise. If it is the second: then if he denies with a witness (shahid), it is al-naqd; but as for his denial without a witness, it is obstinacy (mukabarah) not accepted by consensus. If it is the third: then if he denies with a proof, it is al-mu'aradah; but as for his denial without proof, it is also obstinacy not accepted by consensus.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines munazara and then enumerates the three functions of the questioner (sa'il). The definition describes it as a mutual examination by insight (nazar bil-basira) of the relationship between two things to reveal truth (izharan li-s-sawab). The author then states that each side has functions and there are etiquettes. The questioner's functions are three, corresponding to what he can attack: the premise of the proof, the proof itself, or the conclusion. The first function, munaqada, is an attack on a premise; it can be a simple denial or with a precedent (sanad), and a subtype called al-haqq identifies the exact error. Denying with a proof is rejected as ghasb (usurpation) unless the proof is for the premise itself. The second, naqd, is an attack on the proof by presenting a counterexample (shahid); without a witness it is obstinacy. The third, mu'arada, is an attack on the conclusion by providing an opposing proof; without proof it is obstinacy. The author thus systematically maps the questioner's options and their validity conditions.

Key Terms
الْمُنَاظَرَة (al-munāẓarah): Scholarly disputation: mutual examination by insight to manifest truth. [Note: Stable term; in earlier logic (e.g., Aristotle) it corresponds to dialectic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the genus in the definition of munazara?
A) al-nazar
B) al-basira
C) al-izhar
D) al-sawab

Key Takeaway
Munazara is defined as mutual examination by insight to manifest truth, establishing it as a cooperative truth-seeking process.

?
Write your own definition of munazara in one sentence, ensuring you include the four components (nazar, basira, janibayn, izhar sawab) from the passage.

The Three Functions of the Questioner
Author enumerates the three functions of the questioner and their validity conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three functions of the questioner (munaqada, naqd, mu'arada) and identify the conditions for their validity.

Core Idea
The questioner attacks either the premise, the proof, or the conclusion.

After defining munazara, the author describes the questioner's role. There are exactly three ways to object, each targeting a different component of the argument. Knowing these helps you structure a valid objection in a scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three targets
The questioner can attack the premise (muqaddima), the proof itself (dalil), or the conclusion (madlul). These correspond to the three functions.
2
Understand munaqada (premise denial)
If the questioner denies a premise, it is munaqada. The denial can be simple or with a precedent (sanad). A subtype called al-haqq identifies the exact error.
Denying a premise with a separate proof is not allowed unless that proof justifies the premise itself.
3
Understand naqd (proof refutation by counterexample)
If the questioner attacks the proof itself by presenting a witnessing counterexample (shahid), it is naqd. Without a witness, it is obstinacy (mukabara).
4
Understand mu'arada (counter-argument)
If the questioner attacks the conclusion by providing an alternative proof, it is mu'arada. Without a proof, it is also obstinacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُنَاظَرَةَ: هِيَ النَّظَرُ بِالْبَصِيرَةِ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فِي النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْتَيْنِ؛ إِظْهَارًا لِلصَّوَابِ. وَلِكُلٍّ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ وَظَائِفُ، وَلِلْمُنَاظَرَةِ آدَابٌ . أَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ السَّائِلِ فَثَلَاثَةٌ : الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَالنَّقْضُ، وَالْمُعَارَضَةُ؛ لأَنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يَمْنَعَ مُقَدِّمَةَ الدَّلِيلِ ، أَوِ الدَّلِيلَ نَفْسَهُ، أَوِ الْمَدْلُولَ. فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ مُجَرَّدًا ، أَوْ بِالسَّنَدِ فَهُوَ الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَمِنْهَا نَوْعٌ يُسَمَّى بِالْحَقِّ، وَهُوَ تَعْيِينُ مَوْضِعِ الغَلَطِ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ غَصْبٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعِ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ؛ لاسْتِلْزَامِهِ الْخَبْط ، نَعَمْ قَدْ يَتَوَجَّهُ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ . وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي: فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالشَّاهِدِ فَهُوَ النَّقْضُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا شَاهِدٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ اتِّفَاقًا. وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّالِثُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ الْمُعَارَضَةُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا دَلِيلٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةِ أَيْضًا اتفاقاً .
Know that al-munazarah (scholarly disputation) is: the examination by insight from both sides concerning the relation between two things, for the purpose of manifesting what is correct. For each of the two sides there are functions, and for the disputation there are etiquettes. As for the function of the questioner (al-sa'il), they are three: al-munaqadah (contradiction), al-naqd (refutation by counterexample), and al-mu'aradah (counter-argument); because he either denies a premise of the proof, or the proof itself, or the conclusion. If it is the first: then if he denies it without proof or with a precedent, it is al-munaqadah, and from it there is a type called al-haqq (the truth), which is the determination of the place of error. As for his denial with a proof, it is usurpation (ghasb) not accepted by the verifiers, because it necessitates confusion; however, it may be directed after establishing a proof for that denied premise. If it is the second: then if he denies with a witness (shahid), it is al-naqd; but as for his denial without a witness, it is obstinacy (mukabarah) not accepted by consensus. If it is the third: then if he denies with a proof, it is al-mu'aradah; but as for his denial without proof, it is also obstinacy not accepted by consensus.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage defines munazara and then enumerates the three functions of the questioner (sa'il). The definition describes it as a mutual examination by insight (nazar bil-basira) of the relationship between two things to reveal truth (izharan li-s-sawab). The author then states that each side has functions and there are etiquettes. The questioner's functions are three, corresponding to what he can attack: the premise of the proof, the proof itself, or the conclusion. The first function, munaqada, is an attack on a premise; it can be a simple denial or with a precedent (sanad), and a subtype called al-haqq identifies the exact error. Denying with a proof is rejected as ghasb (usurpation) unless the proof is for the premise itself. The second, naqd, is an attack on the proof by presenting a counterexample (shahid); without a witness it is obstinacy. The third, mu'arada, is an attack on the conclusion by providing an opposing proof; without proof it is obstinacy. The author thus systematically maps the questioner's options and their validity conditions.

Key Terms
السَّائِل (al-sā’il): The questioner in a debate, who objects to the proof or conclusion. [Note: In later adab al-bahth works, the term may be replaced by 'mu'tarid' or 'naqid'.]
الْمُنَاقَضَة (al-munāqaḍah): Denial of a premise, either simple or with a precedent; a type of objection. [Note: Often distinguished from naqd; here it is a specific function.]
النَّقْض (al-naqḍ): Refutation by presenting a counterexample (shahid) that violates the proof. [Note: In logic, naqḍ is often 'reductio ad absurdum'; here it is a specific objection type.]
الْمُعَارَضَة (al-mu‘āraḍah): Counter-argument: denying the conclusion by providing an alternative proof. [Note: In usul al-fiqh, mu'arada is a technical term for conflicting evidence; here it is a debate move.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Munaqada
B) Naqd
C) Mu'arada
D) Mukabara

Key Takeaway
The questioner's three functions target premise, proof, or conclusion, each with specific conditions for validity.

The Answerer's Three Responses in Debate
Author explains how the answerer responds to each type of objection from the questioner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct response of the answerer (mu'allil) to each type of objection (munaqada, naqd, mu'arada) in a jadal debate and apply it in a munazara scenario.

Core Idea
Each objection type demands a specific response from the answerer; knowing which saves the debate.

In a structured dialectical exchange (jadal), the answerer (mu'allil) must respond appropriately to the questioner's objections. This passage from Risalat Adab al-Bahth lays out three distinct objection types—munaqada (denial of a premise), naqd (counterexample), and mu'arada (counter-argument)—and the corresponding required moves for the answerer. Mastering these responses is essential for anyone engaging in scholarly debate within the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Classify the objection
Determine whether the questioner has performed munaqada (denying a premise), naqd (providing a counterexample), or mu'arada (presenting a counter-argument). The passage gives separate rules for each.
The answerer must first recognize the category before responding.
2
For munaqada: choose one of four actions
If the premise is denied, the answerer may: (1) prove the premise with a proof (dalil), (2) point out the premise via tanbih (indication), (3) invalidate the questioner's sanad (precedent) if it is equal to his own, or (4) prove his claim with another proof entirely. Mere denial of the denial is useless.
Tanbih is a lighter form of proof, like an allusion.
3
For naqd: deny the witness or give another proof
If the questioner brings a counterexample (naqd), the answerer has two options: either deny the witness (i.e., show the counterexample does not apply) or establish his claim with a different proof.
The answerer does not need to rebut the witness directly; offering an alternative proof suffices.
4
For mu'arada: engage the opposing proof
When the questioner presents a counter-argument (mu'arada), the answerer must engage with that opposing proof directly. At this point, the answerer effectively becomes the questioner, and the original questioner becomes the answerer for that specific argument.
Roles reverse; the answerer must attack the counter-argument using the same rules.
5
Special case: the transmitter
If the answerer is merely transmitting another scholar's proof (naqil), objections against him are not valid; only verification of the transmission is required. This clarifies that debate rules assume the answerer is defending his own claim.
This prevents unfair attacks on a mere reporter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ الْمُعَلِّلِ ، أَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُنَاقَضَةِ، فَإِثْبَاتُ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ بِالدَّلِيلِ أَوْ بِالتَّنْبِيهِ، أَوْ إِبْطَالُ سَنَدِهِ إِنْ كَانَ مُسَاوِيًا لَهُ؛ إِذْ مَنْعُهُ مُجَرَّدًا غَيْرُ مُفِيدٍ، أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلٍ آخر. وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ النَّقْضِ، فَنَفْيُ شَاهِدِهِ بِالْمَنْعِ، أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلٍ آخَرَ . وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُعَارَضَةِ، فَالتَّعَرُّضُ لِدَلِيل الْمُعَارِض؛ إِذْ يَصِيرُ الْمُعَلِّلُ حِينَئِذٍ كَالسَّائِلِ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ مَنْ يَكُونُ بِصَدَدِ التَّعْلِيلِ قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ مُدَّعِيًا ، بَلْ يَكُونُ نَاقِلًا عَنِ الْغَيْرِ، فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ الْمَنْعُ ، بَلْ يُطْلَبُ مِنْهُ تَصْحِيحُ النَّقْلِ فَقَطْ
As for the function of the answerer (al-mu'allil): in the case of munaqada, he establishes the denied premise with a proof or by pointing out, or he invalidates the questioner's precedent if it is equal to his; for his mere denial is not beneficial, or he establishes his claim with another proof. In the case of naqd, he negates the questioner's witness by denial, or establishes his claim with another proof. In the case of mu'arada, he engages with the opposing proof; for then the answerer becomes like the questioner, and vice versa. Furthermore, whoever is in the position of giving proof may not be a claimant, but a transmitter from others, so no objection is directed at him; rather only verification of the transmission is required from him.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage details the three responses of the mu'allil (the one who provides the proof, i.e., the answerer). For each of the questioner's functions, the answerer has a prescribed response. Against munaqada (denial of a premise), he must either prove the premise with a proof (dalil) or by pointing out (tanbih), or invalidate the questioner's precedent if it is equal (musawi), or prove his claim with a different proof. Mere denial of the denial is useless. Against naqd (counterexample), he either denies the witness (by showing it does not apply) or proves his claim with another proof. Against mu'arada (counter-argument), he must engage with the opposing proof; now the roles reverse — the answerer becomes the questioner attacking that opposing proof. The author also notes a special case: if the answerer is not a claimant (mudda'i) but merely a transmitter (naqil) of someone else's proof, then objections against him are not valid; only verification of the transmission is needed. This clarifies that the debate procedure assumes the answerer is defending his own claim.

Key Terms
الْمُعَلِّل (al-mu‘allil) – the answerer who provides proof
التَّنْبِيه (al-tanbīh) – pointing out or indication as a form of proof
السَّنَد (al-sanad) – precedent or supporting authority used to deny a premise
الْمُنَاقَضَة (al-munāqaḍa) – denial of a premise
النَّقْض (al-naqḍ) – counterexample
الْمُعَارَضَة (al-mu‘āraḍa) – counter-argument

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Engage with the opposing proof, becoming like a questioner
C) Remain silent
D) Deny the questioner's premise

Key Takeaway
The answerer's response must match the objection type: for munaqada prove or invalidate sanad; for naqd deny witness or prove claim anew; for mu'arada engage the counter-argument; a transmitter only needs to verify transmission.

?
Task 1: You are the answerer in a debate. The questioner says: 'Your premise that consensus is a proof is false because Imam Malik did not consider consensus of all scholars valid.' Identify the type of objection (munaqada, naqd, mu'arada) and state the appropriate response from the passage. Write one sentence outlining your action.

Understanding the Two Outcomes of Debate: Ifham and Ilzam
Author defines the two outcomes of disputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ifham and ilzam and identify their roles in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
In debate, if the answerer fails, it is ifham; if the questioner cannot object, it is ilzam.

This passage explains the only two possible ways a debate can end: either the answerer is silenced (ifham) or the questioner is bound (ilzam). Understanding these outcomes helps you track who has the burden of proof in any dialectical encounter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two outcomes
Find the Arabic terms ifham and ilzam in the passage. Read their definitions.
2
Analyse the conditions for ifham
What leads to ifham? The mu'allil (answerer) fails to provide proof for his claim and remains silent. Note that silence signifies defeat.
3
Analyse the conditions for ilzam
What leads to ilzam? The sa'il (questioner) cannot object because the proof reaches a necessarily accepted or conceded premise. The argument becomes binding.
4
Compare and contrast
Ifham is the answerer's failure; ilzam is the questioner's failure. They are not symmetric: one ends by silence, the other by undeniable proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا الَّذِي ذَكَرْنَاهُ طَرِيقُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ . وَأَمَّا مَالُهَا فَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْلُو، إِمَّا أَنْ يَعْجِزَ الْمُعِلِّلُ عَنْ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى مُدَّعَاهُ وَيَسْكُتَ ، فَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإفْحَامُ . أَوْ يَعْجِزَ السَّائِلُ عَنِ التَّعَرُّض لَهُ بِأَنْ يَنْتَهِيَ دَلِيلُ الْمُعَلِّل إِلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ ضَرُورِيَّةِ الْقَبُولِ أَوْ مُسَلَّمَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِلْرَامُ، فَحِينَئِذٍ تَنتَهِي الْمُنَاظَرَةُ؛ إِذْ لَا قُدْرَةَ لَهُمَا عَلَى إِقَامَةِ وَظَائِفِهِمَا لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ. وَأَمَّا آدَابُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ، فَهِيَ أَنَّهُ يَنْبَغِي لِلْمُنَاظِرِ أَنْ يَحْتَرِزَ عَنِ الْإِيجَازِ، وَعَنِ الْإِطْنَابِ، وَعَنِ اسْتِعْمَالِ الْأَلْفَاظِ الْغَرِيبَةِ، وَعَنِ اللَّفْظِ الْمُجْمَلِ، وَلَا بَأْسَ بالاسْتِفْسَارِ، وَعَنِ الدَّخْل قَبْلَ الْفَهْم، وَلَا بَأْسَ بِالْإِعَادَةِ، وَعَنِ التَّعَرُّضِ لِمَا لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِي الْمَقْصُودِ، وَعَنِ الصَّحِكِ وَرَفْعِ الصَّوْتِ وَأَمْثَالِهِمَا، وَعَنِ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ مَعَ أَهْلِ الْمَهَابَةِ وَالاحْتِرَام ، وَأَلَّا يَحْسِبَ الْخَصْمَ حَقِيرًا . هَذَا غَايَةُ مَا يُرَادُ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ، وَمِنَ اللهِ التَّوفِيقُ وَإِلْهَامُ الصَّوَابِ.
This is what we have mentioned as the method of disputation. As for its outcome, it is that either the answerer is unable to establish a proof for his claim and remains silent — that is al-ifham (silencing). Or the questioner is unable to engage with it because the answerer's proof ends at a necessarily acceptable or conceded premise — that is al-ilzam (binding argument). Then the disputation ends, because neither of them has the ability to perform their functions indefinitely. As for the etiquettes of disputation: it is fitting for the disputant to avoid conciseness, prolixity, the use of strange words, and ambiguous expression. There is no harm in asking for clarification, but he should avoid intrusion before understanding. There is no harm in repetition. He should avoid irrelevant matters, laughter, raising the voice, and the like. He should avoid disputing with people of reverence and respect, and he should not consider his opponent as insignificant. This is the utmost that is intended in this chapter. From God is success and inspiration to what is correct.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage concludes the core content by first describing the two possible outcomes of a debate: ifham and ilzam. Ifham occurs when the mu'allil (answerer) is unable to provide proof for his claim and falls silent. Ilzam occurs when the sa'il (questioner) cannot object because the proof ends at a premise that is necessarily accepted (daruriyyat al-qabul) or conceded (musallama). Once this happens, the debate ends because there is no point continuing indefinitely — functions would repeat. Then the author lists the adab (etiquettes) of debate. These include avoiding excessive brevity (ijaz) or prolixity (itnab), strange words (alfaz ghariba), ambiguous terms (lafz mujmal). He permits istifsar (asking for clarification) and i'ada (repetition), but warns against dakhil (interrupting) before understanding. One should avoid irrelevant topics, laughing, raising voice, debating with those deserving reverence, and belittling the opponent. The chapter ends with a prayer for success.

Key Terms
الْإفْحَام
الْإِلْرَام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is ilzam in a debate?
A) The answerer's silence due to inability to prove
B) The questioner's inability to object because the proof is self-evident
C) A type of obstruction
D) The ending of the debate by mutual agreement

Key Takeaway
Ifham and ilzam are the two exclusive outcomes of a disputation, determining who fails in their role.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a debate: A claims 'The world is eternal' (mu'allil). B objects 'But everything that changes is temporal'. A cannot provide proof for eternity and falls silent. Which outcome occurred? (Ifham)
?
Now reverse: A claims 'The world is created' and proves it with 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause, and the world is temporally originated'. B cannot object to the major premise because it is necessarily accepted. Which outcome? (Ilzam)

The Etiquettes (Adab) of Debate According to this Text
Author lists the etiquettes of disputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and apply the etiquettes of debate as prescribed by the author.

Core Idea
Effective debate requires avoiding extremes, ambiguity, and disrespect.

After explaining outcomes, the author lists several adab (etiquettes) that every disputant must observe. These rules ensure that the debate remains productive and respectful. By internalising these, you can conduct or evaluate debates in Islamic sciences more effectively.

Guided Path
1
List the prohibitions
Identify what the author says to avoid: ijaz (conciseness), itnab (prolixity), gharib (strange words), mujmal (ambiguous), dakhil (intrusion before understanding), irrelevant matters, laughing, raising voice, debating with revered people, thinking opponent is insignificant.
2
Note the permissions
He allows istifsar (asking for clarification) and i'ada (repetition).
3
Explain why each etiquette matters
For example, avoiding ambiguous words prevents misunderstanding; avoiding raising voice maintains decorum.
4
Apply to a hypothetical
Imagine a debate where one debater uses jargon. Which etiquette is violated? (Strange words). If he interrupts, which? (Intrusion before understanding).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا الَّذِي ذَكَرْنَاهُ طَرِيقُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ . وَأَمَّا مَالُهَا فَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْلُو، إِمَّا أَنْ يَعْجِزَ الْمُعِلِّلُ عَنْ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى مُدَّعَاهُ وَيَسْكُتَ ، فَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإفْحَامُ . أَوْ يَعْجِزَ السَّائِلُ عَنِ التَّعَرُّض لَهُ بِأَنْ يَنْتَهِيَ دَلِيلُ الْمُعَلِّل إِلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ ضَرُورِيَّةِ الْقَبُولِ أَوْ مُسَلَّمَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِلْرَامُ، فَحِينَئِذٍ تَنتَهِي الْمُنَاظَرَةُ؛ إِذْ لَا قُدْرَةَ لَهُمَا عَلَى إِقَامَةِ وَظَائِفِهِمَا لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ. وَأَمَّا آدَابُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ، فَهِيَ أَنَّهُ يَنْبَغِي لِلْمُنَاظِرِ أَنْ يَحْتَرِزَ عَنِ الْإِيجَازِ، وَعَنِ الْإِطْنَابِ، وَعَنِ اسْتِعْمَالِ الْأَلْفَاظِ الْغَرِيبَةِ، وَعَنِ اللَّفْظِ الْمُجْمَلِ، وَلَا بَأْسَ بالاسْتِفْسَارِ، وَعَنِ الدَّخْل قَبْلَ الْفَهْم، وَلَا بَأْسَ بِالْإِعَادَةِ، وَعَنِ التَّعَرُّضِ لِمَا لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِي الْمَقْصُودِ، وَعَنِ الصَّحِكِ وَرَفْعِ الصَّوْتِ وَأَمْثَالِهِمَا، وَعَنِ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ مَعَ أَهْلِ الْمَهَابَةِ وَالاحْتِرَام ، وَأَلَّا يَحْسِبَ الْخَصْمَ حَقِيرًا . هَذَا غَايَةُ مَا يُرَادُ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ، وَمِنَ اللهِ التَّوفِيقُ وَإِلْهَامُ الصَّوَابِ.
This is what we have mentioned as the method of disputation. As for its outcome, it is that either the answerer is unable to establish a proof for his claim and remains silent — that is al-ifham (silencing). Or the questioner is unable to engage with it because the answerer's proof ends at a necessarily acceptable or conceded premise — that is al-ilzam (binding argument). Then the disputation ends, because neither of them has the ability to perform their functions indefinitely. As for the etiquettes of disputation: it is fitting for the disputant to avoid conciseness, prolixity, the use of strange words, and ambiguous expression. There is no harm in asking for clarification, but he should avoid intrusion before understanding. There is no harm in repetition. He should avoid irrelevant matters, laughter, raising the voice, and the like. He should avoid disputing with people of reverence and respect, and he should not consider his opponent as insignificant. This is the utmost that is intended in this chapter. From God is success and inspiration to what is correct.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage concludes the core content by first describing the two possible outcomes of a debate: ifham and ilzam. Ifham occurs when the mu'allil (answerer) is unable to provide proof for his claim and falls silent. Ilzam occurs when the sa'il (questioner) cannot object because the proof ends at a premise that is necessarily accepted (daruriyyat al-qabul) or conceded (musallama). Once this happens, the debate ends because there is no point continuing indefinitely — functions would repeat. Then the author lists the adab (etiquettes) of debate. These include avoiding excessive brevity (ijaz) or prolixity (itnab), strange words (alfaz ghariba), ambiguous terms (lafz mujmal). He permits istifsar (asking for clarification) and i'ada (repetition), but warns against dakhil (interrupting) before understanding. One should avoid irrelevant topics, laughing, raising voice, debating with those deserving reverence, and belittling the opponent. The chapter ends with a prayer for success.

Key Terms
اللَّفْظ الْمُجْمَل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Using ambiguous expressions
B) Asking for clarification
C) Laughing during the debate
D) Using strange words

Key Takeaway
A well-conducted debate requires clear language, patience, and respect for the opponent.

Duties of the Mu'allil for Three Objection Types
The author explains the third type of objection and the duties of the mu'allil in response to each type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of objections in a jadal debate and state the appropriate duty of the mu'allil for each.

Core Idea
In Islamic debate, the mu'allil's response depends on the type of objection: munaqadah, naqd, or mu'aradah.

This lesson details the third type of objection introduced in the previous page—mu'aradah (counter-argument with evidence)—and then explains the responsibilities of the mu'allil (proof-giver) when facing each objection type. Understanding these rules is essential for conducting orderly and productive debates in the Islamic intellectual tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objection types
The passage lists three valid objection types: munaqadah (exposing contradiction), naqd (counterexample), and mu'aradah (opposing proof). Also note mukabarah (obstinacy without evidence), which is not accepted.
2
Distinguish mu'aradah from mukabarah
Mu'aradah is a denial accompanied by evidence; mukabarah is denial without evidence. The latter is rejected by consensus.
3
Determine duty for each objection
For munaqadah: establish the denied premise with evidence, or point out reasoning, or invalidate opponent's support, or provide a new proof. For naqd: deny the counterexample or provide a new proof. For mu'aradah: address the opponent's proof directly, at which point roles reverse.
4
Handle the transmitter case
If the mu'allil is not the claimant but a transmitter, denial is not directed at him; he only needs to verify the accuracy of the transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّالِثُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ الْمُعَارَضَةُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا دَلِيلٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةِ أَيْضًا اتفاقاً .
وَأَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ الْمُعَلِّلِ ، أَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُنَاقَضَةِ، فَإِثْبَاتُ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ بِالدَّلِيلِ أَوْ بِالتَّنْبِيهِ، أَوْ إِبْطَالُ سَنَدِهِ إِنْ كَانَ مُسَاوِيًا لَهُ؛ إِذْ مَنْعُهُ مُجَرَّدًا غَيْرُ مُفِيدٍ، أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلٍ آخر.
وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ النَّقْضِ، فَنَفْيُ شَاهِدِهِ بِالْمَنْعِ، أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلٍ آخَرَ .
وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُعَارَضَةِ، فَالتَّعَرُّضُ لِدَلِيل الْمُعَارِض؛ إِذْ يَصِيرُ الْمُعَلِّلُ حِينَئِذٍ كَالسَّائِلِ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ.
ثُمَّ إِنَّ مَنْ يَكُونُ بِصَدَدِ التَّعْلِيلِ قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ مُدَّعِيًا ، بَلْ يَكُونُ نَاقِلًا عَنِ الْغَيْرِ، فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ الْمَنْعُ ، بَلْ يُطْلَبُ مِنْهُ تَصْحِيحُ النَّقْلِ فَقَطْ
And if it is the third: if he denies with evidence, then it is mu'aradah. And as for his denial without evidence, it is obstinacy which is not listened to by consensus. And as for the duty of the mu'allil: during munaqadah, it is to establish the denied premise with evidence or by pointing out, or to invalidate its support if it is equivalent to it; because mere denial is not beneficial, or to establish his claim with another proof. And during naqd, it is to negate the counterexample by denial, or to establish his claim with another proof. And during mu'aradah, it is to address the proof of the opponent; for at that point the mu'allil becomes like the questioner, and vice versa. Then, whoever is engaged in giving proof may not be the claimant, but rather a transmitter from others, so denial does not apply to him; rather, he is only required to verify the transmission.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage first clarifies the third type of objection (the first two are presumably on earlier pages). The third type is when the opponent denies with evidence — this is called mu'aradah (counter-argument). However, denial without any evidence is mere obstinacy (mukabarah) and is not admissible by consensus. The author then details the duties of the mu'allil (the one providing the proof) in three situations. In munaqadah (pointing out a contradiction), the mu'allil must either prove the denied premise with evidence or by pointing out a reasoning, or he can invalidate the basis of the opponent's objection if it is equivalent to his own proof, since mere denial is useless; or else he can provide an entirely new proof for his claim. In naqd (refutation by counterexample), the mu'allil can either deny that the counterexample actually undermines his proof, or he can provide a different proof for his claim. In mu'aradah (counter-argument), the mu'allil must directly address the opponent's counter-proof, at which point the roles reverse: the mu'allil becomes like the questioner and must respond to the opponent's evidence. Finally, the author notes a subtlety: a person giving proof may not be the original claimant but is merely transmitting from someone else. In that case, the questioner cannot simply deny the transmission; instead, the mu'allil is only required to verify the accuracy of the transmission.

Key Terms
الْمُعَلِّل
الْمُنَاقَضَة
النَّقْض
الْمُعَارَضَة
الْمُكَابَرَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the duty of the mu'allil when the opponent commits mu'aradah?
A) Deny the opponent's claim without evidence
B) Establish the denied premise with evidence
C) Address the opponent's proof directly
D) Provide a new proof unrelated to the opponent's objection

Key Takeaway
The mu'allil's response varies: establish the premise for munaqadah, negate the counterexample for naqd, or address the opposite proof for mu'aradah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a munazara on the permissibility of Qiyas, the opponent objects by saying: 'Qiyas leads to different rulings, so it is invalid' – this is munaqadah. What should the mu'allil do according to this passage?
?
If the opponent provides a counterexample (naqd) that the Prophet (saw) performed an action without Qiyas, what are the mu'allil's options?

Outcomes and Etiquette of Debate
Author explains debate outcomes and etiquettes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ifham and ilzam and list the etiquette rules for a respectful debate.

Core Idea
A debate ends when one party is silenced or convinced; etiquette ensures productive discourse.

In this final portion of the debate methodology, the author explains how a debate properly concludes and what rules govern the conduct of the debaters. Understanding these outcomes and etiquettes is essential for anyone who wishes to engage in scholarly discourse within Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two outcomes
Read the Arabic: إِمَّا أَنْ يَعْجِزَ الْمُعِلِّلُ عَنْ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ ... أَوْ يَعْجِزَ السَّائِلُ. The passage presents two possibilities at the end of a debate.
2
Define ifham and ilzam
Ifham occurs when the mu'allil fails to prove his claim and falls silent. Ilzam occurs when the questioner cannot object because the proof is based on a necessary or conceded premise.
Memorize which party fails in each.
3
List the ten etiquettes
From the text, enumerate: avoid brevity/verbosity, strange words, ambiguous expressions; don't interrupt before understanding; avoid irrelevant topics; no laughter/loud voice; respect authority; don't belittle opponent. Also allowed: seeking clarification and repetition.
There is no harm in استفسار (seeking clarification) and إعادة (repetition).
4
Apply to a scenario
Imagine a debate where the mu'allil uses strange terms and the sa'il raises his voice. Identify which etiquettes are violated.
Strange words and raising voice are prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا الَّذِي ذَكَرْنَاهُ طَرِيقُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ . وَأَمَّا مَالُهَا فَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْلُو، إِمَّا أَنْ يَعْجِزَ الْمُعِلِّلُ عَنْ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى مُدَّعَاهُ وَيَسْكُتَ ، فَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإفْحَامُ .
أَوْ يَعْجِزَ السَّائِلُ عَنِ التَّعَرُّض لَهُ بِأَنْ يَنْتَهِيَ دَلِيلُ الْمُعَلِّل إِلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ ضَرُورِيَّةِ الْقَبُولِ أَوْ مُسَلَّمَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِلْرَامُ، فَحِينَئِذٍ تَنتَهِي الْمُنَاظَرَةُ؛ إِذْ لَا قُدْرَةَ لَهُمَا عَلَى إِقَامَةِ وَظَائِفِهِمَا لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ.
وَأَمَّا آدَابُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ، فَهِيَ أَنَّهُ يَنْبَغِي لِلْمُنَاظِرِ أَنْ يَحْتَرِزَ عَنِ الْإِيجَازِ، وَعَنِ الْإِطْنَابِ، وَعَنِ اسْتِعْمَالِ الْأَلْفَاظِ الْغَرِيبَةِ، وَعَنِ اللَّفْظِ الْمُجْمَلِ، وَلَا بَأْسَ بالاسْتِفْسَارِ، وَعَنِ الدَّخْل قَبْلَ الْفَهْم، وَلَا بَأْسَ بِالْإِعَادَةِ، وَعَنِ التَّعَرُّضِ لِمَا لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِي الْمَقْصُودِ، وَعَنِ الصَّحِكِ وَرَفْعِ الصَّوْتِ وَأَمْثَالِهِمَا، وَعَنِ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ مَعَ أَهْلِ الْمَهَابَةِ وَالاحْتِرَام ، وَأَلَّا يَحْسِبَ الْخَصْمَ حَقِيرًا .
هَذَا غَايَةُ مَا يُرَادُ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ، وَمِنَ اللهِ التَّوفِيقُ وَإِلْهَامُ الصَّوَابِ.
This that we have mentioned is the method of debate. As for its conclusion, it is that either the mu'allil is unable to establish proof for his claim and falls silent — that is al-ifham (silencing). Or the questioner is unable to object because the mu'allil's proof ends at a necessarily accepted or conceded premise — that is al-ilzam (convincing). At that point the debate ends, for they have no ability to continue performing their duties indefinitely. As for the etiquettes of debate, it is that the debater should avoid brevity, verbosity, using strange words, and ambiguous expressions; there is no harm in seeking clarification, but avoid interrupting before understanding; there is no harm in repetition; avoid discussing matters irrelevant to the purpose; avoid laughing, raising the voice, and similar; avoid debating with people of awe and respect; and he should not consider the opponent insignificant. This is the extent of what is desired in this chapter, and from Allah is success and inspiration of correctness.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of debate methodology. The author first defines the two possible outcomes. If the mu'allil (proof-giver) cannot provide support for his claim and falls silent, that is ifham — the questioner has silenced him. If the questioner cannot find any flaw because the mu'allil's proof rests on a self-evident or conceded premise, that is ilzam — the questioner is convinced. In either case, the debate ends because neither party can continue infinitely. Then the author lists ten etiquettes: the debater should avoid excessive brevity or verbosity, strange words, and ambiguous expressions. He may ask for clarification but should not interrupt before understanding. He may repeat but should avoid irrelevant topics. He must not laugh, raise his voice, or behave in an undignified manner. He should avoid debating with those who command awe and respect, and he should not belittle his opponent. The author ends with a prayer for divine assistance. These etiquettes ensure that the debate remains focused, respectful, and productive.

Key Terms
الإفْحَام
الإلْزَام
آدَاب الْمُنَاظَرَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Ifham occurs when the mu'allil fails, ilzam when the sa'il fails

Key Takeaway
The debate ends when one party cannot continue: either the mu'allil (ifham) or the sa'il (ilzam); proper etiquette preserves the academic nature of the discussion.

?

Definition of Munazara and the Three Functions of the Questioner
Author defines munazara and lists the three functions of the questioner with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three functions of the questioner (sa'il) in munazara and match each function to its target (premise, proof, or conclusion).

Core Idea
Munazara is a structured insight-based debate to reveal truth, with three defined objection types.

This passage from Adab al-Bahth defines the science of debate (munazara) and then breaks down the specific role of the questioner. The author classifies every possible objection into one of three types, each with its own target and conditions. Understanding these categories is the first step to mastering the etiquette of debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of munazara
Locate the first sentence: it defines munazara as an insight-based examination from both sides of the relationship between two things to manifest truth. Note the key components: examination (nazar), insight (basira), both sides, relationship, and aim of truth.
2
Recognize the three targets of objection
The passage says the sa'il may block either the premise (muqaddima), the proof (dalil), or the conclusion (madlul). List these three parts of any proof.
3
Match each target to its corresponding function
When the target is a premise, the function is munaqada; when the proof, naqd; when the conclusion, mu'arada. This mapping is explicit in the text.
4
Note the special subtype 'al-haqq'
Within munaqada, there is a type called 'al-haqq' which means specifying the exact location of error. This is a refined form of objection.
5
Apply the classification to a sample objection
Consider an objection: 'Your premise that all ijtihad is valid is false because the Companions sometimes corrected each other.' Which target does it attack? It attacks the premise, so it would be munaqada if supported by a sanad or simple denial.
This step solidifies the mapping.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمُ أَنَّ الْمُنَاظَرَةَ : هِيَ النَّظَرُ بِالْبَصِيرَةِ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فِي النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْتَيْنِ؛ إِظْهَارًا لِلصَّوَابِ. وَلِكُلِّ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ وَظَائِفُ، وَلِلْمُنَاظَرَةِ آدَابٌ . أَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ السَّائِلِ فَثَلَاثَةٌ : (۱) الْمُنَاقَضَةُ . (۲) وَالنَّقْضُ . (۳) وَالْمُعَارَضَةُ . - لِأَنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يَمْنَعَ : (۱) مُقَدِّمَةَ الدَّلِيلِ. (۲) أَوِ الدَّلِيلَ نَفْسَهُ. (٣) أَوِ الْمَدْلُول . فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ: - فَإِنْ مَنَعَ مُجَرَّدًا، أَوْ بِالسَّنَدِ فَهُوَ الْمُنَاقَضَةُ . - وَمِنْهَا نَوْعٌ يُسَمَّى بِالْحَقِّ، وَهُوَ تَعْيِينُ مَوْضِع الغَلَط . - وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ غَصْبٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعِ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ ؛ لِاسْتِلْزَامِهِ الْخَبْطَ . نَعَمْ قَدْ يَتَوَجَّهُ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ . وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي : - فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالشَّاهِدِ فَهُوَ النَّقْضُ. - وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا شَاهِدٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ اتِّفَاقًا . وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّالِثُ : - فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ الْمُعَارَضَةُ. - وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلا دَلِيلِ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ أَيْضًا اتِّفَاقًا .
Know that munazara (debate) is: the insight-based examination from both sides of the relationship between the two things, in order to manifest the truth. And for each of the two sides there are functions, and for munazara there are etiquettes. As for the function of the questioner, it is three: (1) contradiction (munaqada), (2) nullification (naqd), and (3) counterargument (mu'arada). Because either he may prevent: (1) the premise of the proof, (2) the proof itself, or (3) the conclusion. If it is the first: then if he prevents it merely, or with a supporting authority, it is contradiction (munaqada). And of it there is a type called 'al-haqq' (the truth), which is specifying the place of error. As for preventing it with a proof, it is usurpation (ghasb) not accepted by the verifiers, because it entails confusion. Yes, it may be directed after establishing the proof on that prevented premise. If it is the second: then if he prevents it with a counterexample (shahid), it is nullification (naqd). As for preventing it without a counterexample, it is obstinacy (mukabarah), not accepted by consensus. If it is the third: then if he prevents it with a proof, it is counterargument (mu'arada). As for preventing it without a proof, it is also obstinacy not accepted by consensus.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines the art of munazara (debate) and then classifies the functions of one party: the questioner (sa'il), who is the one who raises challenges. The definition states that munazara is an examination using insight (basira) from both sides concerning the relationship between two things, with the goal of manifesting truth (sawab). The author notes that each side has specific functions, and the debate itself has etiquettes.

The sa'il has exactly three functions: munaqada (contradiction), naqd (nullification), and mu'arada (counterargument). These correspond to the three possible targets of the sa'il's objection: the premise (muqaddima) of the proof, the proof (dalil) itself, or the conclusion (madlul).

If the sa'il blocks the premise, then if he does so simply (without proof) or by citing a supportive authority (sanad), it is called munaqada. A subtype of munaqada is 'al-haqq', which means pinpointing the exact location of the error. However, if he blocks the premise by bringing a proof against it, that is ghasb (usurpation) and is not accepted by verifiers because it leads to confusion (khabat). Exceptionally, this may be allowed after he has already established a proof for that same premise.

If the sa'il blocks the proof itself, then if he brings a counterexample (shahid), it is naqd (nullification). Without a shahid, it is obstinacy (mukabara) and unanimously rejected.

If the sa'il blocks the conclusion, then if he brings a proof against it, it is mu'arada (counterargument). Without a proof, it is again obstinacy and rejected.

السائل
المناقضة
النقض
المعارضة
المقدمة
الدليل
المدلول

A) Munaqada
B) Naqd
C) Mu'arada
D) Mukabara

Key Takeaway
The questioner's three functions correspond to the three parts of any proof: premise, proof, and conclusion, each requiring a specific form of support.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which function (munaqada, naqd, mu'arada) applies: In a debate about whether qiyas is a valid source of law, a Hanafi debater objects: 'Your proof that qiyas is invalid because it is not mentioned in the Quran is flawed. Here is a case where the Companions used qiyas (the case of 'Umar and the intoxicating nabidh).' Which function is this, and why?
?
Classify the following objection as valid munaqada or invalid ghasb: The questioner says to the proof-giver: 'Your premise that every form of worship must have a textual basis is false because the Prophet performed some actions without explicit command.' Is this a valid objection? If so, what type?

Valid vs. Invalid Objections: Conditions for Acceptance
Author details conditions for each function of the questioner, distinguishing valid from invalid objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given objection in a debate is valid (munaqada, naqd, mu'arada) or invalid (mukabara, ghasb) based on the presence of sanad, shahid, or dalil.

Core Idea
An objection is only valid if it comes with the appropriate evidence: sanad for premises, shahid for proofs, dalil for conclusions.

This lesson zooms into the conditions the author sets for each type of objection. Not every challenge to a premise or proof is acceptable; some are considered obstinacy (mukabara) or usurpation (ghasb). Learning these distinctions is essential for engaging in legitimate scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Review the three targets and valid objections
Remind yourself: premise -> munaqada (with or without sanad), proof -> naqd (must have shahid), conclusion -> mu'arada (must have dalil).
2
Identify invalid forms for each target
For premise: objecting with a proof is ghasb (usurpation) except after first proving that premise. For proof: objecting without shahid is mukabara. For conclusion: objecting without dalil is mukabara.
The exception for premise is nuanced: the objector may bring a proof only after he has established that proof on the disputed premise.
3
Understand the rationale for invalidity
Objecting to a premise with a proof leads to confusion (khabat) because it mixes levels of argument. Objecting without support is obstinacy.
4
Apply the conditions to sample objections
Classify each objection as valid (munaqada/naqd/mu'arada) or invalid (mukabara/ghasb) based on presence of sanad, shahid, or dalil.
Practice with examples from fiqh or kalam debates.

Types of Objection in Debate: Premise, Evidence, and Conclusion
Author classifies objections according to target: premise, evidence, conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given objection in a munazara as munaqada, naqd, or mu'arada and determine its validity according to the rules.

Core Idea
Not all objections are equal; the target and the backing determine validity.

In formal Islamic debate (munazara), the questioner (sa'il) can object to the proponent's (mu'allil) statement in three ways: attacking the premise, the evidence, or the conclusion. This lesson parses the precise terminology and conditions for each type, so you can evaluate objections in real debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three targets of objection
The passage lists three possible objects of attack: the premise (muqaddima), the evidence (dalil), and the conclusion (madlul). These correspond to the first, second, and third cases in the text.
The targets are hierarchical: premise supports evidence, evidence supports conclusion.
2
Distinguish the two sub-types per target
For each target, the author distinguishes between an objection with proper backing (proof, chain, counter-example) and one without. The former is given a specific name (munaqada, naqd, mu'arada); the latter is always rejected as obstinacy (mukabara) or usurpation (ghasb).
The exception: objecting to a premise with proof is ghasb and generally rejected, but becomes acceptable after the premise is proven.
3
Learn the technical terms
Memorize: al-munaqada (objection to premise without/with chain), al-naqd (objection to evidence with counter-example), al-mu'arada (objection to conclusion with proof). Note that al-haqq is a subtype of munaqada that pinpoints the error.
These terms are specific to the munazara tradition and are not used in general logic.
4
Recognize invalid forms
Objecting to the premise with a proof is ghasb (usurpation) and not accepted by verifiers because it leads to circularity. Objecting to evidence without a counter-example, or to conclusion without a proof, is mukabara (obstinacy) and unanimously rejected.
The author notes that ghasb becomes permissible if the proponent first proves the objectionable premise.
5
Apply the classification to an example
Given a short exchange, identify the target of the objection and whether it has proper backing. For instance: 'Proponent: Wine is forbidden because it is an intoxicant. Objector: But nabidh is also intoxicating and it is not forbidden.' This objector attacks the evidence (the premise that all intoxicants are forbidden) by providing a counter-example (nabidh), which is a valid naqd.
Always check if the objection is backed by evidence appropriate to its target.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ: - فَإِنْ مَنَعَ مُجَرَّدًا، أَوْ بِالسَّنَدِ فَهُوَ الْمُنَاقَضَةُ . - وَمِنْهَا نَوْعٌ يُسَمَّى بِالْحَقِّ، وَهُوَ تَعْيِينُ مَوْضِع الغَلَط . - وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ غَصْبٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعِ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ ؛ لِاسْتِلْزَامِهِ الْخَبْطَ . نَعَمْ قَدْ يَتَوَجَّهُ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ . وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي : - فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالشَّاهِدِ فَهُوَ النَّقْضُ. - وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا شَاهِدٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ اتِّفَاقًا . وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّالِثُ : - فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ الْمُعَارَضَةُ. - وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلا دَلِيلِ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ أَيْضًا اتِّفَاقًا .
If it is the first [i.e., the premise]: then if he objects without evidence or with attribution, it is contradictory opposition (al-munaqada). Some of it is a type called al-haqq, which is pinpointing the error. As for objecting with a proof, it is usurpation (ghasb) not accepted by the verifiers because it entails confusion. However, that may be permitted after establishing proof for that objected premise. If it is the second [i.e., the evidence]: then if he objects with a counter-example, it is rebuttal (al-naqd). As for objecting without a counter-example, it is obstinacy (mukabara) unanimously not accepted. If it is the third [i.e., the conclusion]: then if he objects with a proof, it is counter-argument (al-mu'arada). As for objecting without a proof, it is also obstinacy unanimously not accepted.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of objection (man') in a dialectical debate according to the target of the attack: (1) the premise (muqaddima), (2) the evidence (dalil), or (3) the conclusion (madlul). For each, the author distinguishes two sub-types: valid and invalid. When the premise is objected to, if the objection is without proof or with mere chain (sanad), it is called contradictory opposition (munaqada), which includes the subtype 'al-haqq' (pinpointing error). If the objection itself is supported by a proof, it is called ghasb (usurpation) and is generally not accepted because it leads to circularity. However, the author notes an exception: after proving the premise, such an objection becomes valid. When the evidence is targeted, if the opponent brings a counter-example (shahid), it is called naqd (rebuttal); without a counter-example, it is mere obstinacy (mukabara). When the conclusion is targeted, if the opponent brings a counter-argument (dalil), it is called mu'arada (counter-argument); without a proof, it is again obstinacy. This classification sets the rules for acceptable objections in formal debate.

Key Terms
منع (man'): Objection raised by the questioner (sa'il) against the proponent's (mu'allil) statement, classified by the target: premise, evidence, or conclusion. [Note: In earlier logic manuals, man' often refers only to objecting to a premise; here it is generalized to three targets.]
المناقضة (al-munaqada): An objection that targets the premise, either without evidence or with a chain (sanad); includes pinpointing error (al-haqq).
النقض (al-naqd): A rebuttal that targets the evidence by providing a counter-example (shahid).
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): A counter-argument that targets the conclusion by providing an alternative proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a debater objects to the evidence provided by the opponent by bringing a counter-example, what is this type of objection called?
A) Munaqada
B) Naqd
C) Mu'arada
D) Mukabara

Key Takeaway
The validity of an objection in munazara depends not only on its target but also on whether it is supported by appropriate evidence (counter-example for evidence, proof for conclusion, etc.).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a munazara on the permissibility of photography, the proponent says: 'Photography is forbidden because it is a form of image-making (taswir).' The objector responds: 'But photography is not the same as hand-made images; it is a reflection of reality.' Which target does this objection attack? Is it a valid objection according to the text? Justify your answer.
?
Construct a short debate exchange (two lines) where the objector commits 'mukabara' (obstinacy) by attacking the conclusion without proof. Explain why it is invalid.

Duties of the Proponent in Responding to Objections
Author details the required responses (wazifa) for the proponent when facing each type of objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct response for the proponent (mu'allil) when facing munaqada, naqd, or mu'arada in a debate.

Core Idea
The mu'allil has three response strategies, and his role changes in mu'arada.

This passage from al-Risala al-Adabiyya outlines the precise duties of the proponent (mu'allil) when the opponent raises different types of objections. Understanding these duties is crucial for conducting rigorous debates according to the rules of adab al-bahth.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three objection types
The passage groups objections into three categories: munaqada (objection to a premise), naqd (counter-example), and mu'arada (counter-argument). Each requires a different set of responses.
2
Extract responses for each type
For munaqada, the proponent can: (a) prove the premise, (b) invalidate the opponent's chain, or (c) prove the claim by another proof. For naqd, the proponent can: (a) deny the counter-example by objecting, or (b) prove the claim by another proof. For mu'arada, the proponent must engage the opponent's proof directly.
Note that some options overlap across types.
3
Note the special case of the transmitter
If the proponent is merely transmitting a report (naqil), not asserting a claim, objection does not apply. Only verification of transmission is required.
This protects reporters from being challenged on the content itself.
4
Summarize the proponent's role shift
In mu'arada, the proponent becomes like a questioner (sa'il), having to rebut the opponent's counter-argument. This role reversal is key to the dialectical process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ الْمُعَلِّلِ : (۱) أَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُنَاقَضَةِ : ١ - فَإِثْبَاتُ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ بِالدَّلِيلِ أَوْ بِالتَّنْبِيهِ. ٢- أَوْ إِبْطَالُ سَنَدِهِ إِنْ كَانَ مُسَاوِيًا لَهُ؛ إِذْ مَنْعُهُ مُجَرَّدًا غَيْرُ مُفِيدٍ . - أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلِ آخَرَ . (۲) وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ النَّقْضِ : ١ - فَنَفْيُ شَاهِدِهِ بِالْمَنْعِ. ٢- أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلِ آخَرَ (۳) وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُعَارَضَةِ : - فَالتَّعَرُّضُ لِدَلِيلِ الْمُعَارِضِ ؛ إِذْ يَصِيرُ الْمُعَلِّلُ حِينَئِذٍ كَالسَّائِلِ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ مَنْ يَكُونُ بِصَدَدِ التَّعْلِيلِ قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ مُدَّعِيًا ، بَلْ يَكُونُ نَاقِلا فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ الْمَنْعُ، بَلْ يُطْلَبُ مِنْهُ تَصْحِيحُ النَّقْلِ فَقَطْ . هَذَا الَّذِي ذَكَرْنَاهُ طَريقُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ.
As for the function of the proponent (mu'allil): (1) In the case of contradictory opposition (munaqada): 1. Proving the objected premise by evidence or by pointing out; 2. Or invalidating the opponent's chain if it matches his own; for objecting without evidence is useless; 3. Or proving his own claim by another proof. (2) In the case of rebuttal (naqd): 1. Denying the counter-example by objecting to it; 2. Or proving his own claim by another proof. (3) In the case of counter-argument (mu'arada): Engaging the opponent's proof; for then the proponent becomes like a questioner, and vice versa. Furthermore, one who is engaged in providing proof may not be advancing a claim but merely transmitting, so objection does not apply to him; only correction of the transmission is required. This is what we have mentioned as the path of debate.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage explains what the proponent (mu'allil) must do when faced with each type of objection from the previous classification. When the opponent commits munaqada (objecting to a premise), the proponent has three options: prove the premise with evidence or reminder, dismantle the opponent's chain (sanad) if it is equivalent, or prove his own claim by another argument. When the opponent commits naqd (counter-example), the proponent can either deny the counter-example by objecting to it, or prove his claim by another evidence. When the opponent commits mu'arada (counter-argument), the proponent must engage the opponent's proof, effectively switching roles to become like the questioner. The author also notes a special case: if the proponent is merely transmitting a reported claim (naqil), not asserting it, then objection is not directed at him; only verification of the transmission is needed. The passage concludes by stating that this is the method of debate.

Key Terms
المعطل
سند
شاهد
ناقل

Key Terms from Source Passage
