Outcomes of Debate: Incapacitation and Conclusive Proof
Author defines the two possible end results of a debate: ifham (incapacitation) and ilzam (conclusive proof).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ifham and ilzam in a dialectical exchange and identify which outcome applies given the state of the argument.

Core Idea
A debate ends either in defeat or in an irrefutable proof.

This passage defines the two possible end states of a debate in the Islamic dialectical tradition. Understanding these outcomes is crucial for evaluating who has won and why the debate cannot continue indefinitely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two outcomes
The author lists two possibilities. Locate them in the Arabic: (1) ifham and (2) ilzam.
Look for the terms الإفحام and الإلزام.
2
Define ifham
Ifham occurs when the proponent (mu'allil) is unable to provide evidence for his claim and falls silent. This is a defeat by inability.
The key phrase is 'يعجز المعلل عن إقامة الدليل على دعواه ويسكت'.
3
Define ilzam
Ilzam occurs when the questioner (sa'il) cannot challenge the argument because the proponent's proof reaches a premise that is necessarily acceptable (daruriyyat al-qabul) or conceded (musallama).
The key phrase is 'ينتهي دليل المعطل إلى مقدمة ضرورية القبول أو مسلمة'.
4
Understand debate termination
In either case, the debate ends because neither party can continue their roles indefinitely. If the proponent fails, silence ends it. If the questioner is cornered by an irrefutable premise, further objection is impossible.
The text says: 'فحينئذ تنتهي المناظرة، إذ لا قدرة لهما على إقامة وظائفهما لا إلى نهاية'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا مَالُهَا : فَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْلُو: (۱) إِمَّا أَنْ يَعْجِزَ الْمُعِلِّلُ عَنْ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيل عَلَى مُدَّعَاهُ وَيَسْكُتَ. فَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِفْحَامُ. (٢) أَوْ يَعْجِزَ السَّائِلُ عَنِ التَّعَرُّضِ لَهُ بِأَنْ يَنْتَهِيَ دَلِيلُ الْمُعَلِّلِ إِلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ : ضَرُورِيَّةِ الْقَبُولِ، أَوْ مُسَلَّمَةٍ. وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِلْزَامُ. فَحِينَئِذٍ تَنْتَهِي الْمُنَاظَرَةُ، إِذْ لَا قُدْرَةَ لَهُمَا عَلَى إِقَامَةِ وَظَائِفِهِمَا لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ .
As for its outcome: it is either (1) that the proponent is unable to provide evidence for his claim and falls silent; that is incapacitation (ifham). Or (2) that the questioner is unable to engage with it because the proponent's evidence reaches a premise that is necessarily acceptable or conceded; that is conclusive proof (ilzam). At that point, the debate ends, since neither can perform their functions indefinitely.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage defines the two possible outcomes of a debate. The first, ifham, occurs when the proponent (mu'allil) cannot provide proof for his claim and remains silent—he is defeated. The second, ilzam, occurs when the questioner (sa'il) cannot challenge the proponent's argument because the premises are either self-evident (daruriyya al-qabul) or conceded by the questioner (musallama). In either case, the debate ends because no further productive exchange is possible—neither party can continue their roles indefinitely. This structure maintains that a debate should not be infinite regress but has a clear termination point.

Key Terms
الإفحام (al-ifham): The outcome where the proponent is unable to prove his claim and falls silent; defeat by inability.
الإلزام (al-ilzam): The outcome where the questioner is forced to accept the proponent's argument because it rests on undeniable or conceded premises.
ضرورية القبول (daruriyyat al-qabul): A premise that must be accepted because it is self-evident or axiomatic.
مسلمة (musallama): A premise that is conceded or agreed upon by both parties in the debate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ifham
B) Ilzam
C) Mukabara
D) Munaqada

Key Takeaway
The two outcomes of a debate — ifham (incapacitation) and ilzam (conclusive proof) — ensure that every dialectical exchange has a clear termination point.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following scenario: A proponent argues that the prohibition of wine is due to its intoxication. The questioner concedes that intoxicants are prohibited but disputes that wine is an intoxicant. Which outcome would apply if the proponent fails to prove wine is an intoxicant? Explain.

Construct a brief dialectical exchange where the outcome would be ilzam. Specify the premises that are self-evident or conceded.

Three Rules of Debate Etiquette
Author lists three rules of decorum for a debater.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the three etiquettes of debate: avoiding excessive brevity, excessive length, and strange terminology.

Core Idea
Clarity and moderation in speech are the keys to effective debate.

This passage from the Risala fi Adab al-Bahth presents three concise rules for any debater. These rules are not arbitrary; they address common pitfalls that obscure the argument and hinder understanding. We'll unpack each rule and see why the author warns against them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first rule: excessive brevity (al-ijaz)
The author warns against being too brief, as it can lead to ambiguity or omission of necessary details. A debater must balance conciseness with completeness.
2
Identify the second rule: excessive length (al-itnab)
Verbosity can bore the audience and distract from the core argument. The debater should be thorough but not prolix.
3
Identify the third rule: strange terminology (al-alfaz al-ghariba)
Using obscure words confuses the opponent and listeners. The language should be accessible to all parties.
4
Understand the underlying principle
All three rules aim at achieving clarity (wuduh) and effectiveness in debate. Moderation is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا آدَابُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ : فَهِيَ أَنَّهُ يَنْبَغِي لِلْمُنَاظِرِ أَنْ يَحْتَرِزَ : (۱) عَنِ الْإِيجاز. (۲) وَعَنِ الْإِطْنَابِ . (۳) وَعَنِ اسْتِعْمَالِ الْأَلْفَاظِ الْغَرِيبَةِ.
As for the etiquette of debate: it is that the debater should guard against (1) excessive brevity, (2) excessive length, and (3) the use of strange terms.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage provides three practical etiquettes (adab) for a debater. First, avoid excessive brevity (ijaz) which can lead to ambiguity or omission of necessary details. Second, avoid excessive elaboration (itnab) which can bore the audience and distract from the core argument. Third, avoid using strange or unfamiliar words (alfaz ghariba) which may confuse the opponent or listeners. The author implies that the debater should aim for clarity and moderation in speech, using language that is accessible to all parties. These rules ensure that the debate remains focused and understandable.

Key Terms
الإيجاز
الإطناب
الألفاظ الغريبة

Outcomes and Etiquette of Debate (Ifham, Ilzam, and Adab)
Author concludes debate rules: methods of attack, outcomes (ifham/ilzam), and etiquette.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two outcomes of a debate (ifham and ilzam) and apply the etiquette rules to evaluate a given debate scenario.

Core Idea
Debate ends either by silencing (ifham) or binding (ilzam) — knowing which is genuine separates the trained mutakallim from the amateur.

This passage by the author wraps up the essential rules of formal debate (munazara). You have learned the moves (man', mu'arada) and now see their culmination in two distinct outcomes. More importantly, you learn the etiquette (adab) that transforms raw argumentation into disciplined, respectful scholarly discourse. In Islamic sciences, from usul al-fiqh to kalam, debates are won not by shouting but by adhering to these norms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two outcomes of debate
Find the sections headed by 'مالها' (its outcome). Extract the definitions of ifham (silencing) and ilzam (binding). Notice how each outcome terminates the debate because one party cannot continue.
Ifham: mu'allil fails to prove and falls silent. Ilzam: sa'il cannot challenge a necessary or conceded premise.
2
Trace the role reversal in mu'arada
Read the part about mu'arada (counter-argument). When the opponent presents a counter-argument, the original proponent (mu'allil) becomes the questioner. Understand why this happens: the burden of proof shifts.
This ensures fairness – each side must defend its own evidence.
3
List the nine etiquette rules
Go through the adab section systematically. Group them: rules about speech (avoid excessive brevity/prolixity, rare words, ambiguous language), about behavior (no laughter, loud voice), and about attitude (not belittling opponent, avoiding debate with those in authority).
The author also permits asking for clarification and repeating – these are tools to ensure understanding.
4
Apply the etiquette to a hypothetical debate
Imagine a debate about the permissibility of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in usul al-fiqh. Pick one etiquette rule (e.g., 'avoid rare words') and explain why violating it would harm the debate's integrity.
For instance, using obscure usuli terminology might confuse the opponent and prevent a fair exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَنَفْيُ شَاهِدِهِ بِالْمَنْعِ. أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلِ آخَرَ. وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُعَارَضَةِ : فَالتَّعَرُّضُ لِدَلِيلِ الْمُعَارِضِ ؛ إِذْ يَصِيرُ الْمُعَلِّلُ حِينَئِذٍ كَالسَّائِلِ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ مَنْ يَكُونُ بِصَدَدِ التَّعْلِيلِ قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ مُدَّعِيًا ، بَلْ يَكُونُ نَاقِلا فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ الْمَنْعُ، بَلْ يُطْلَبُ مِنْهُ تَصْحِيحُ النَّقْلِ فَقَطْ . هَذَا الَّذِي ذَكَرْنَاهُ طَريقُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ. وَأَمَّا مَالُهَا : فَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْلُو: إِمَّا أَنْ يَعْجِزَ الْمُعِلِّلُ عَنْ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيل عَلَى مُدَّعَاهُ وَيَسْكُتَ. فَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِفْحَامُ. أَوْ يَعْجِزَ السَّائِلُ عَنِ التَّعَرُّضِ لَهُ بِأَنْ يَنْتَهِيَ دَلِيلُ الْمُعَلِّلِ إِلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ ضَرُورِيَّةِ الْقَبُولِ، أَوْ مُسَلَّمَةٍ. وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِلْزَامُ. فَحِينَئِذٍ تَنْتَهِي الْمُنَاظَرَةُ، إِذْ لَا قُدْرَةَ لَهُمَا عَلَى إِقَامَةِ وَظَائِفِهِمَا لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ . وَأَمَّا آدَابُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ : فَهِيَ أَنَّهُ يَنْبَغِي لِلْمُنَاظِرِ أَنْ يَحْتَرِزَ : عَنِ الْإِيجاز. وَعَنِ الْإِطْنَابِ . وَعَنِ اسْتِعْمَالِ الْأَلْفَاظِ الْغَرِيبَةِ. وَعَنِ اللَّفْظِ الْمُجْمَلِ، وَلَا بَأْسَ بِالِاسْتِفْسَارِ. وَعَنِ الدَّخْلِ قَبْلَ الفَهم، وَلَا بَأسَ بِالْإِعَادَةِ . وَعَنِ التَّعَرُّضِ لِمَا لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِي الْمَقْصُودِ. وَعَنِ الصَّحِكِ وَرَفْعِ الصَّوْتِ وَأَمْثَالِهِمَا. وَعَنِ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ مَعَ أَهْلِ الْمَهَابَةِ وَالِاحْتِرَامِ. وَأَلَّا يَحْسِبَ الْخَصْمَ حَقِيرًا . هَذَا غَايَةُ مَا يُرَادُ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ وَمِنَ اللهِ التَّوفِيقُ وَإِلْهَامُ الصَّوَاب.
So denying his evidence by disallowance (man'), or proving his claim by another evidence. As for when there is opposition (mu'arada): addressing the evidence of the opponent, because the one presenting the proof (mu'allil) then becomes like the questioner, and vice versa. Then, whoever is engaged in providing proof may not be a claimant (mudda'i) but rather a transmitter (naqil), so disallowance is not directed at him; rather, he is only required to verify the transmission. This is the method of debate we have mentioned. As for its outcome: it does not go beyond either the mu'allil fails to establish evidence for his claim and falls silent—that is silencing (ifham); or the questioner fails to address it, because the mu'allil's evidence ends at a premise that is necessarily acceptable (daruriyya al-qabool) or conceded (musallama)—that is binding (ilzam). At that point the debate ends, because they have no ability to perform their functions indefinitely. As for the etiquette of debate: the debater should avoid: excessive brevity; excessive prolixity; using rare words; ambiguous expression—though asking for clarification is fine; getting into the topic before understanding—though repetition is fine; addressing matters not relevant to the purpose; laughter, raising the voice, and the like; debating with people of awe and reverence; and not to consider the opponent contemptible. This is the utmost desired in this chapter, and from Allah is success and inspiration of correctness.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion of debate (munazara) by summarizing permissible moves, outcomes, and proper conduct. First, the author reiterates the main methods of attack: either refuting the opponent's evidence by 'man'' (disallowing its premise or validity) or proving one's own claim with a different evidence. In case of a direct counter-argument (mu'arada), the roles reverse: the original proponent (mu'allil) becomes the questioner and must defend his evidence, while the objector assumes the burden of proof. A special case arises when the mu'allil is merely transmitting a position rather than personally claiming it; then the opponent cannot simply reject (man') but must verify the accuracy of the transmission. This entire set of rules constitutes the 'method of debate.' The author then turns to the possible outcomes: (1) ifham (silencing) occurs when the mu'allil is unable to produce a valid proof for his claim and falls silent; (2) ilzam (binding) occurs when the questioner cannot challenge the mu'allil's evidence because it rests on a necessarily accepted or conceded premise—here the questioner is compelled to concede. At either point the debate terminates, as neither party can continue indefinitely. Finally, nine points of etiquette are listed: avoid excessive brevity and prolixity, rare words, ambiguous language (though clarification is allowed), entering the topic before understanding (though repetition is allowed), irrelevant tangents, laughter and loudness, debating with those who command awe (as it may be disrespectful), and never belittling the opponent. The passage ends with a pious invocation, indicating the author's intention to provide the essential rules of this science.

Key Terms
إفحام
إلزام
منع
معارضة
آداب المناظرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Ifham
C) Man'
D) Mu'arada

Key Takeaway
The two outcomes of debate (ifham and ilzam) and the nine etiquette rules together ensure that scholarly debate remains a pursuit of truth, not a contest of egos.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Six Prohibitions in Debate (Points 4-9)
Author lists prohibitions in debate from point four to nine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the six prohibitions in debate from points 4-9 of the text.

Core Idea
Six rules that safeguard respectful and productive debate.

This passage presents six specific prohibitions that a debater must observe. These rules ensure that the discussion stays on track, clear, and respectful. Understanding them is essential for anyone who wishes to engage in serious intellectual exchange within the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the prohibitions
Read the Arabic text and translation. List each prohibition from 4 to 9. Note the key Arabic terms.
Use the key terms list as a memory aid.
2
Understand the justification
For each prohibition, think about why it would harm a debate. For example, ambiguous language (mujmal) leads to confusion; interrupting before understanding (dakhl qabl al-fahm) prevents proper response.
3
Identify the exceptions
Note that the text allows istifsar (asking for clarification) and iʿāda (repeating). These are not prohibited but encouraged as remedies.
4
Apply to a real debate scenario
Consider a debate between two fuqaha about the ruling of a new transaction. Imagine one debater uses ambiguous analogies. Which prohibition does that violate? How should the other respond?

(٤) وَعَنِ اللَّفْظِ الْمُجْمَلِ، وَلَا بَأْسَ بِالِاسْتِفْسَارِ. (٥) وَعَنِ الدَّخْلِ قَبْلَ الفَهم، وَلَا بَأسَ بِالْإِعَادَةِ . (٦) وَعَنِ التَّعَرُّضِ لِمَا لَا دَخْلَ لَهُ فِي الْمَقْصُودِ. (6) And avoid bringing up what is irrelevant to the intended purpose. (7) And avoid laughing, raising one's voice, and the like. (8) And avoid debating with those of dignity and respect. This passage continues the list of prohibited actions in debate. The author enumerates prohibitions four through nine. The fourth prohibits ambiguous speech (al-lafz al-mujmal), although asking for clarification (al-istifsar) is allowed. The fifth prohibits interrupting before understanding (al-dakhl qabl al-fahm), but repeating is allowed. The sixth prohibits addressing irrelevant issues. The seventh prohibits laughing and raising the voice. The eighth prohibits debating with people of dignity and respect (ahl al-mahabah wa al-ihtiram). The ninth prohibits considering the opponent as contemptible (haqiran). These are all manners that preserve the propriety of debate.

Definition of Munazarah and the Questioner's Three Functions
Author opens treatise, defines munazarah, and lists questioner's three functions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define munazarah and list the three functions of the questioner with a brief description of each.

Core Idea
Munazarah is a disciplined mutual examination for truth, not a free-for-all debate.

This passage opens a treatise on the science of adab al-bahth, which governs scholarly disputation in the Islamic tradition. The author defines the subject—munazarah—and then enumerates the three tools available to the questioner to test the validity of the responder's proof. This lesson will help you internalize the definition and grasp how these functions relate to the structure of a proof.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of munazarah
Identify the key components: mutual insight-based consideration (النظر بالبصيرة من الجانبين), concerning the relation between two things (في النسبة بين الشيئين), aiming to manifest truth (إظهارا للصواب).
This definition emphasizes that munazarah is not mere argumentation but a structured inquiry.
2
Distinguish the questioner from the responder
Note that the author separates the functions of each side. The questioner (السائل) challenges and tests the proof; the responder (المجيب) defends it. This lesson focuses on the questioner.
3
Memorize the three functions of the questioner
The text lists three: المناقضة (contradiction), النقض (refutation), and المعارضة (counter-argument). Commit these Arabic terms and their translations to memory.
These are the only functions the author assigns to the questioner in this opening.
4
Understand the basis for the three functions
The author explains the rationale: the questioner either attacks a premise of the proof (المقدمة), the proof itself (الدليل), or the conclusion (المدلول). Match each function to its target: munaqadah targets a premise, naqd targets the proof, mu'aradah targets the conclusion.
This logical correspondence helps in applying the correct function in a real debate.
5
Recognize the subtype 'al-haqq'
Within munaqadah, there is a type called 'الحق' (the truth/correction), which means specifying the exact place of error in the opponent's reasoning. This is a precise form of contradiction.
This term is distinctive to this author; later works may not use it.

بِسْمِ اللَّهِ الرَّحْمَنِ الرَّحِيمِ
أَحْمَدُكَ اللَّهُمَّ يَا مُجِيبَ كُلِّ سَائِلٍ، وَأَصَلِّي عَلَى نَبِيِّكَ الْمَبْعُوثِ بِأَقْوَى الدَّلَائِلِ، وَعَلَى آلِهِ وَأَصْحَابِهِ الْمُتَوَسِّلِينَ بِأَعْظَمِ الْوَسَائِلِ، مَا جَرَى الْبَحْثُ بَيْنَ الْمُجِيبِ وَالسَّائِلِ.
وَبَعْدُ، فَهَذِهِ رِسَالَةٌ لَخَصْتُهَا فِي عِلْمِ الْآدَابِ، مُجْتَنِبًا عَنْ طَرَفَي الِاقْتِصَادِ: الْإِخْلَالِ وَالْإِطْنَابِ، وَالله أَسْأَلُ أَنْ يَنْفَعَ بِهَا مَعَاشِرَ الطُّلابِ، وَمَا تَوْفِيقِي إِلَّا بِاللهِ، عَلَيْهِ تَوَكَّلْتُ، وَإِلَيْهِ الْمَآبُ.
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُنَاظَرَةَ : هِيَ النَّظَرُ بِالْبَصِيرَةِ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فِي النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْئَيْنِ؛ إِظْهَارًا لِلصَّوَابِ.
وَلِكُلٍّ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ وَظَائِفُ، وَلِلْمُنَاظَرَةِ آدَابٌ.
أَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ السَّائِلِ فَثَلَاثَةُ : الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَالنَّقْضُ، وَالْمُعَارَضَةُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يَمْنَعَ مُقَدِّمَةَ الدَّلِيل، أَوِ الدَّلِيلَ نَفْسَهُ، أَوِ الْمَدْلُولَ.
فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ مُجَرَّدًا، أَوْ بِالسَّنَدِ فَهُوَ الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَمِنْهَا نَوْعٌ يُسَمَّى بِالْحَقِّ، وَهُوَ تَعْيِينُ مَوْضِع الْغَلَطِ.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. I praise You, O Allah, O Answerer of every questioner, and I send blessings upon Your Prophet sent with the strongest proofs, and upon his family and companions who seek intercession through the greatest means, as long as discussion occurs between the responder and the questioner. To proceed: this is a treatise I have summarized in the science of disputation, avoiding both extremes of conciseness and prolixity. I ask Allah to benefit the students with it. My success is only from Allah; upon Him I rely, and to Him is the return. Know that munazarah (disputation) is: insight-based consideration from both sides concerning the relation between two things, for the manifestation of what is correct. Each side has functions, and disputation has etiquettes. As for the function of the questioner, it is three: contradiction (munaqadah), refutation (naqd), and counter-argument (mu'aradah); because he either prevents a premise of the proof, or the proof itself, or the conclusion. If it is the first: then if he prevents it absolutely, or with evidence, it is munaqadah. Among its types is that called al-haqq (the truth/correction), which is specifying the place of error.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage opens the treatise with a conventional Islamic invocation (basmalah, hamdalah, salawat) and then states that this is a summary work on the science of adab (dialectical disputation). The author explains that he has struck a balance between too little and too much, then invokes divine help. He immediately defines the subject: munazarah is a mutual examination with inner insight from both parties concerning the relationship between two things, aiming to reveal the truth. He then informs us that each side (questioner and responder) has specific duties, and the process has etiquettes. He focuses first on the questioner (sa'il). The questioner's function is threefold: munaqadah (contradiction), naqd (refutation), and mu'aradah (counter-argument). These correspond to three possible attacks on the proof: preventing a premise, attacking the proof's very structure, or attacking the conclusion. The first type—preventing a premise—if done without or with evidence, is called munaqadah. A subtype of this is called 'al-haqq', which means pinpointing the exact location of error in the opponent's reasoning. This lays the groundwork for the technical tools of disputation, all grounded in the Islamic scholarly tradition.

The Three Functions of the Questioner in Munazarah
Author defines munazarah and lists three questioner functions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the three functions of the questioner (munaqadah, naqd, mu'araqah) is being used based on what is denied in a disputation.

Core Idea
The questioner's objection targets the premise, the evidence, or the conclusion.

In any formal disputation (munazarah), the questioner (sa'il) has a structured role: to object to the respondent's argument. But not all objections are the same. This passage lays out three distinct functions, each defined by what part of the argument is being denied. Understanding these categories is the first step to mastering the art of scholarly debate in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of munazarah
The author defines munazarah as a bilateral critical examination to manifest truth. Note the purpose: إظهارًا للصواب (manifesting truth).
2
Recognize the three objects of denial
The questioner can deny: a premise (مقدمة الدليل), the evidence itself (الدليل), or the conclusion (المدلول). This gives the three functions.
3
Match each function to its object
Denying a premise → munaqadah; denying the evidence → naqd; denying the conclusion → mu'araqah. Practice identifying which is which.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُنَاظَرَةَ : هِيَ النَّظَرُ بِالْبَصِيرَةِ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فِي النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْئَيْنِ؛ إِظْهَارًا لِلصَّوَابِ. وَلِكُلٍّ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ وَظَائِفُ، وَلِلْمُنَاظَرَةِ آدَابٌ . أَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ السَّائِلِ فَثَلَاثَةُ : الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَالنَّقْضُ، وَالْمُعَارَضَةُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يَمْنَعَ مُقَدِّمَةَ الدَّلِيل، أَوِ الدَّلِيلَ نَفْسَهُ، أَوِ الْمَدْلُولَ . فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ مُجَرَّدًا ، أَوْ بِالسَّنَدِ فَهُوَ الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَمِنْهَا نَوْعٌ يُسَمَّى بِالْحَقِّ، وَهُوَ تَعْيِينُ مَوْضِع الغَلَطِ . وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ غَصْبٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعِ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ؛ لِاسْتِلْزَامِهِ الْخَبْطَ ، نَعَمْ قَدْ يَتَوَجَّهُ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيل عَلَى تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ. وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي: فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالشَّاهِدِ فَهُوَ النَّقْضُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا شَاهِدٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ اتِّفَاقًا. وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّالِثُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ الْمُعَارَضَةُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا دَلِيلٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ أَيْضًا اتِّفَاقًا
Know that disputation (al-munazarah) is: critical examination with insight by both parties concerning the relationship between two things, in order to manifest correctness. Each of the two parties has functions, and disputation has etiquettes. As for the function of the questioner, it is three: contradiction (al-munaqadah), refutation (al-naqd), and counter-argument (al-mu'araqah); because he either denies a premise of the evidence, or the evidence itself, or the conclusion. If the first: if he denies (the premise) simply, or with a chain (sanad), then it is al-munaqadah; and among it is a type called al-haqq, which is pinpointing the place of error. As for his denial with evidence, it is usurpation (ghasb) not heard according to the verifiers, because it entails confusion; yes, that may be considered after establishing evidence for that denied premise. If the second: if he denies with a witness (shahid), then it is al-naqd; as for his denial without a witness, it is obstinacy (mukabarah) not heard by agreement. If the third: if he denies with evidence, then it is al-mu'araqah; as for his denial without evidence, it is obstinacy not heard as well by agreement.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines the art of disputation (munazarah) and then focuses on the functions of the questioner (sa'il). The author first defines munazarah as a bilateral critical examination aimed at manifesting truth. Then he states that each side has functions and there are etiquettes. The core of the passage details the three functions of the questioner: (1) al-munaqadah (contradiction): objecting to a premise. This can be either a simple denial (without justification) or a denial with a chain (sanad). Among munaqadah is a subtype called al-haqq, which is precisely specifying the location of error. However, if the questioner denies the premise by providing evidence (dalil), it is considered ghasb (usurpation) and unacceptable to the verifiers because it leads to confusion (khabt), unless he first proves the premise itself. (2) al-naqd (refutation): objecting to the evidence itself. This is valid only if he provides a counter-witness (shahid); otherwise it is mere obstinacy (mukabarah) unanimously rejected. (3) al-mu'araqah (counter-argument): objecting to the conclusion by providing counter-evidence. Without evidence, it is also rejected as obstinacy. The passage thus establishes a rigorous standard: objections must be substantiated appropriately. For a fiqhi example: in a debate about whether qiyas (analogy) is a valid proof, if the questioner denies the premise 'The Prophet used qiyas' by simply saying 'I reject that premise,' that is munaqadah. If he brings a chain (e.g., a hadith that contradicts the claim), it is still munaqadah but with sanad. If he instead attacks the evidence itself by producing a counter-example (e.g., a case where qiyas was not used), that is naqd. If he accepts the evidence but offers a different conclusion based on a counter-argument (e.g., 'Even if the Prophet used qiyas, there is a stronger evidence for rejecting it'), that is mu'araqah.

Key Terms
المناظرة
السائل
المناقضة
النقض
المعارضة
الغصب
الشاهد
السند

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) al-naqd
C) al-mu'araqah
D) al-ghasb

Key Takeaway
The three functions of the questioner are defined by which part of the argument is denied: premise (munaqadah), evidence (naqd), or conclusion (mu'araqah).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a scenario: The respondent argues 'Khamr is forbidden because it intoxicates (dalil).' The questioner replies: 'I deny that intoxication is the effective cause (illah) of prohibition.' Identify which function of the questioner this is and explain your reasoning based on the passage.
?
Construct your own example for each of the three functions using a fiqhi or kalami issue.

Valid and Invalid Objections: Conditions for Munaqadah, Naqd, and Mu'araqah
Author details conditions for valid and invalid objections for each function

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an objection as valid or invalid based on whether it meets the required backing (sanad, shahid, or dalil) as described in the passage.

Core Idea
An objection without proper support is either ghasb or mukabarah, both rejected.

The previous lesson introduced the three functions of the questioner. Now we examine the conditions that make an objection acceptable. The author distinguishes between valid objections (with sanad, shahid, or dalil) and invalid ones (ghasb and mukabarah). This lesson unpacks those conditions and explains why verifiers reject unsupported objections.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three functions
Munaqadah (deny premise), naqd (deny evidence), mu'araqah (deny conclusion).
2
Identify the valid form of each
For munaqadah: either simple denial or denial with sanad (chain). For naqd: must be with a shahid (witness/example). For mu'araqah: must be with a dalil (evidence).
3
Recognize the invalid forms
For munaqadah: denying a premise with evidence (dalil) is ghasb, not accepted unless the premise is proven. For naqd: denial without shahid is mukabarah. For mu'araqah: denial without dalil is also mukabarah.
4
Apply the classification
Practise by giving examples and asking if the objection is valid or invalid and why.

اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُنَاظَرَةَ : هِيَ النَّظَرُ بِالْبَصِيرَةِ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فِي النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْئَيْنِ؛ إِظْهَارًا لِلصَّوَابِ. وَلِكُلٍّ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ وَظَائِفُ، وَلِلْمُنَاظَرَةِ آدَابٌ . أَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ السَّائِلِ فَثَلَاثَةُ : الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَالنَّقْضُ، وَالْمُعَارَضَةُ؛ لِأَنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يَمْنَعَ مُقَدِّمَةَ الدَّلِيل، أَوِ الدَّلِيلَ نَفْسَهُ، أَوِ الْمَدْلُولَ . فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ مُجَرَّدًا ، أَوْ بِالسَّنَدِ فَهُوَ الْمُنَاقَضَةُ، وَمِنْهَا نَوْعٌ يُسَمَّى بِالْحَقِّ، وَهُوَ تَعْيِينُ مَوْضِع الغَلَطِ . وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ غَصْبٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعِ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ؛ لِاسْتِلْزَامِهِ الْخَبْطَ ، نَعَمْ قَدْ يَتَوَجَّهُ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيل عَلَى تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ. وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي: فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالشَّاهِدِ فَهُوَ النَّقْضُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا شَاهِدٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ اتِّفَاقًا. وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّالِثُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ الْمُعَارَضَةُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا دَلِيلٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ أَيْضًا اتِّفَاقًا
Know that disputation (al-munazarah) is: critical examination with insight by both parties concerning the relationship between two things, in order to manifest correctness. Each of the two parties has functions, and disputation has etiquettes. As for the function of the questioner, it is three: contradiction (al-munaqadah), refutation (al-naqd), and counter-argument (al-mu'araqah); because he either denies a premise of the evidence, or the evidence itself, or the conclusion. If the first: if he denies (the premise) simply, or with a chain (sanad), then it is al-munaqadah; and among it is a type called al-haqq, which is pinpointing the place of error. As for his denial with evidence, it is usurpation (ghasb) not heard according to the verifiers, because it entails confusion; yes, that may be considered after establishing evidence for that denied premise. If the second: if he denies with a witness (shahid), then it is al-naqd; as for his denial without a witness, it is obstinacy (mukabarah) not heard by agreement. If the third: if he denies with evidence, then it is al-mu'araqah; as for his denial without evidence, it is obstinacy not heard as well by agreement.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage defines the art of disputation (munazarah) and then focuses on the functions of the questioner (sa'il). The author first defines munazarah as a bilateral critical examination aimed at manifesting truth. Then he states that each side has functions and there are etiquettes. The core of the passage details the three functions of the questioner: (1) al-munaqadah (contradiction): objecting to a premise. This can be either a simple denial (without justification) or a denial with a chain (sanad). Among munaqadah is a subtype called al-haqq, which is precisely specifying the location of error. However, if the questioner denies the premise by providing evidence (dalil), it is considered ghasb (usurpation) and unacceptable to the verifiers because it leads to confusion (khabt), unless he first proves the premise itself. (2) al-naqd (refutation): objecting to the evidence itself. This is valid only if he provides a counter-witness (shahid); otherwise it is mere obstinacy (mukabarah) unanimously rejected. (3) al-mu'araqah (counter-argument): objecting to the conclusion by providing counter-evidence. Without evidence, it is also rejected as obstinacy. The passage thus establishes a rigorous standard: objections must be substantiated appropriately. For a fiqhi example: in a debate about whether qiyas (analogy) is a valid proof, if the questioner denies the premise 'The Prophet used qiyas' by simply saying 'I reject that premise,' that is munaqadah. If he brings a chain (e.g., a hadith that contradicts the claim), it is still munaqadah but with sanad. If he instead attacks the evidence itself by producing a counter-example (e.g., a case where qiyas was not used), that is naqd. If he accepts the evidence but offers a different conclusion based on a counter-argument (e.g., 'Even if the Prophet used qiyas, there is a stronger evidence for rejecting it'), that is mu'araqah.

المناقضة

المعارضة

المكابرة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the questioner denies a premise with evidence (dalil) in the case of munaqadah?
A) It is valid munaqadah
B) It is ghasb and not accepted
C) It is naqd
D) It is mu'araqah

Key Takeaway
For an objection to be valid, munaqadah may be simple or with sanad; naqd requires a shahid; mu'araqah requires a dalil. Otherwise, it is rejected as ghasb or mukabarah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For each of the following objections, determine whether it is valid or invalid according to the passage, and if invalid, name the type (ghasb or mukabarah): (a) Denying the premise 'The Prophet performed analogy' by saying 'I have evidence that he did not.' (b) Denying the evidence 'The hadith of Mu'adh' by providing a counter-example. (c) Denying the conclusion 'Therefore nabidh is forbidden' by saying 'That is wrong' without evidence.
?

Classification of Objections in Dialectic
Classification of objections into naqd, mu'āraḍa, and obstinacy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish valid objections (naqd and mu'āraḍa) from invalid obstinacy in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Objecting without evidence or witness is obstinacy, not debate.

In Islamic dialectic, not every objection is permissible. The passage lays down the rules: objecting with evidence is only allowed after proper proof; objecting with a witness constitutes naqd (refutation), while objecting with proof is mu'āraḍa (counterargument). Objecting without support is mere obstinacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
The passage discusses the permissibility of objection (manʿ) to a premise in a debate. It assumes a structure where the questioner objects to a part of the evidence.
2
Recognize the two valid cases
The second case (implied first is not detailed) is when the questioner objects with a witness (shāhid) – this is naqd. The third case is when he objects with evidence (dalīl) – this is mu'āraḍa.
Naqd is a counterexample; mu'āraḍa is a counter-argument with proof.
3
Identify invalid objections
Objecting without a witness in the second case, or without evidence in the third case, is considered obstinacy (mukābara) and is not accepted unanimously.
4
Note the exception for objecting with evidence
The passage states that objecting with evidence (manʿ bi al-dalīl) is usurpation (ghaṣb) unless it follows establishing the evidence for the prevented premise. So it is conditionally valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ غَصْبٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعٍ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ؛ لِاسْتِلْزَامِهِ الْخَبْطِ، نَعَمْ قَدْ يَتَوَجَّهُ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيل عَلَى تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ. وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي: فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالشَّاهِدِ فَهُوَ النَّقْضُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا شَاهِدٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ اتِّفَاقًا. وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّالِثُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ الْمُعَارَضَةُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا دَلِيلٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ أَيْضًا اتِّفَاقًا. وَأَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ الْمُعَلِّلِ، أَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُنَاقَضَةِ، فَإِثْبَاتُ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ بِالدَّلِيلِ أَوْ بِالتَّنْبِيهِ، أَوْ إِبْطَالُ سَنَدِهِ إِنْ كَانَ مُسَاوِيًا لَهُ؛ إِذْ مَنْعُهُ مُجَرَّدًا غَيْرُ مُفِيدٍ، أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلِ آخَرَ. وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ النَّقْضِ، فَنَفْيُ شَاهِدِهِ بِالْمَنْعِ، أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلٍ آخَرَ. وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُعَارَضَةِ، فَالتَّعَرُّضُ لِدَلِيلِ الْمُعَارِضِ؛ إِذْ يَصِيرُ الْمُعَلِّلُ حِينَئِذٍ كَالسَّائِلِ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ مَنْ يَكُونُ بِصَدَدِ التَّعْلِيلِ قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ مُدَّعِيًا، بَلْ يَكُونُ نَاقِلًا عَنِ الْغَيْرِ، فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ الْمَنْعُ، بَلْ يُطْلَبُ مِنْهُ تَصْحِيحُ النَّقْلِ فَقَطْ.
As for preventing (objecting) with evidence, it is usurpation not listened to among the verifiers, because it entails confusion. Yes, that may be directed after establishing the evidence for that prevented premise. If it is the second case: if he prevents with a witness, then it is refutation (naqd); but his preventing without a witness is obstinacy not listened to unanimously. If it is the third: if he prevents with evidence, then it is counterargument (mu'āraḍa); but his preventing without evidence is obstinacy not listened to also unanimously. As for the duty of the one providing evidence (al-mu'allil): at the time of contradiction (munāqaḍa), it is to establish the prevented premise by evidence or by pointing out, or to invalidate his support if it is equal to it; since preventing it alone is not useful, or to establish his claim by another evidence. At the time of refutation (naqd), it is to deny his witness by preventing, or to establish his claim by another evidence. At the time of counterargument (mu'āraḍa), it is to take on the evidence of the opponent; for then the mu'allil becomes like the questioner, and vice versa. Then, whoever is in the position of providing evidence may not be a claimant, but rather a transmitter from someone else; so prevention is not directed at him, rather he is only required to verify the transmission.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage defines the proper responses of the mu'allil (the one who provides evidence) when faced with three types of objections from the questioner. The passage first distinguishes between valid and invalid forms of objection: objecting with evidence is not accepted unless it follows establishing a proof; objecting with a witness is naqd (refutation by counterexample); objecting without witness is obstinacy; objecting with proof is mu'āraḍa (counterargument); objecting without proof is also obstinacy. Then it explains the mu'allil's duties. When faced with munāqaḍa (contradiction), the mu'allil must either prove the disputed premise, show its evidence, nullify the opponent's proof if it is equivalent, or prove his claim with another evidence. When faced with naqd, he must deny the opponent's counterexample by prevention or prove his claim with another evidence. When faced with mu'āraḍa, he must engage the opponent's proof, becoming like the questioner. Finally, if the mu'allil is merely transmitting from another (e.g., quoting a scholar), he is not subject to prevention; he only needs to verify the transmission. In Islamic debate, for example, if one argues 'Wine is forbidden because it is an intoxicant' and the opponent objects with 'Nabidh is an intoxicant but not forbidden' (naqd), the mu'allil must either deny the witness (e.g., 'Nabidh is not an intoxicant') or prove his claim with another evidence ('Nabidh is forbidden').

Key Terms
النقض
المعارضة
المكابرة
الشاهد

According to this passage, what is the ruling on objecting with evidence (manʿ bi al-dalīl)?
A) It is always valid.
B) It is usurpation unless it follows establishing the evidence for the prevented premise.
C) It is obstinacy not listened to.
D) It is called naqd.

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, only objections with a witness (naqd) or with evidence (mu'āraḍa) are valid; unsupported objections are dismissed as obstinacy.

6 min
The Duties of the Mu'allil in Response to Objections
Duties of the mu'allil for each objection type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct response of the mu'allil when faced with contradiction, refutation, or counterargument.

Core Idea
The mu'allil must tailor his response to the type of objection: prove, deny, or engage the opponent's proof.

Once an objection is classified, the mu'allil has specific duties. This passage details three strategies: for munāqaḍa (contradiction), he must establish the premise or invalidate the opponent's support; for naqd, he must deny the witness or provide new evidence; for mu'āraḍa, he must engage the opponent's proof, becoming like the questioner. Also, if he is merely transmitting, he only needs to verify the transmission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage lists three situations: contradiction (munāqaḍa), refutation (naqd), and counterargument (mu'āraḍa). Each requires a different response.
2
Learn the response for munāqaḍa
When the questioner contradicts a premise, the mu'allil must either establish the premise by evidence or pointing out, or invalidate the opponent's support if it is equal to his own, or prove his claim with another evidence.
Munāqaḍa is not defined elsewhere in this passage, but it seems to be a direct contradiction.
3
Learn the response for naqd
When faced with a counterexample (naqd), the mu'allil must either deny the witness (prevent the shāhid) or prove his claim with another evidence.
4
Learn the response for mu'āraḍa
When faced with a counterargument (mu'āraḍa), the mu'allil must take on the opponent's evidence, i.e., refute it, thereby becoming like the original questioner.
5
Note the special case of the transmitter
If the mu'allil is merely transmitting from another authority, he is not subject to prevention; he only needs to verify the correctness of the transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ غَصْبٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعٍ عِنْدَ الْمُحَقِّقِينَ؛ لِاسْتِلْزَامِهِ الْخَبْطِ، نَعَمْ قَدْ يَتَوَجَّهُ ذَلِكَ بَعْدَ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيل عَلَى تِلْكَ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ. وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي: فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالشَّاهِدِ فَهُوَ النَّقْضُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا شَاهِدٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ اتِّفَاقًا. وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّالِثُ : فَإِنْ مَنَعَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَهُوَ الْمُعَارَضَةُ، وَأَمَّا مَنْعُهُ بِلَا دَلِيلٍ فَهُوَ مُكَابَرَةٌ غَيْرُ مَسْمُوعَةٍ أَيْضًا اتِّفَاقًا. وَأَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ الْمُعَلِّلِ، أَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُنَاقَضَةِ، فَإِثْبَاتُ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ بِالدَّلِيلِ أَوْ بِالتَّنْبِيهِ، أَوْ إِبْطَالُ سَنَدِهِ إِنْ كَانَ مُسَاوِيًا لَهُ؛ إِذْ مَنْعُهُ مُجَرَّدًا غَيْرُ مُفِيدٍ، أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلِ آخَرَ. وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ النَّقْضِ، فَنَفْيُ شَاهِدِهِ بِالْمَنْعِ، أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلٍ آخَرَ. وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُعَارَضَةِ، فَالتَّعَرُّضُ لِدَلِيلِ الْمُعَارِضِ؛ إِذْ يَصِيرُ الْمُعَلِّلُ حِينَئِذٍ كَالسَّائِلِ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ مَنْ يَكُونُ بِصَدَدِ التَّعْلِيلِ قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ مُدَّعِيًا، بَلْ يَكُونُ نَاقِلًا عَنِ الْغَيْرِ، فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ الْمَنْعُ، بَلْ يُطْلَبُ مِنْهُ تَصْحِيحُ النَّقْلِ فَقَطْ.
As for preventing (objecting) with evidence, it is usurpation not listened to among the verifiers, because it entails confusion. Yes, that may be directed after establishing the evidence for that prevented premise. Then, whoever is in the position of providing evidence may not be a claimant, but rather a transmitter from someone else; so prevention is not directed at him, rather he is only required to verify the transmission.

D) Verify the transmission only.

Key Takeaway
The mu'allil's duty varies with objection type: for contradiction, establish; for refutation, deny or prove; for counterargument, engage the opponent's proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Suppose you are the mu'allil in a debate about the necessity of repentance from minor sins. The questioner objects with mu'āraḍa: 'But there is evidence that minor sins are forgiven without repentance.' What is your duty? Outline your response.
?
2. In a debate about the transmission of a hadith, the mu'allil says 'I am only narrating from Imam Malik.' What is the proper response from the questioner according to this passage?

The Mu'allil's Functions in Munazara
Author describes the mu'allil's functions at each stage and the two possible endings of debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the appropriate response of a mu'allil at each of the three stages of debate (munaqada, taqdiyya, mu'arada) and recognize the exception for transmitters.

Core Idea
A mu'allil must adapt his defense to the type of attack: denial, invalidation, or counter-argument.

In classical Islamic debate (munazara), the mu'allil is the one who provides evidence for a claim. This lesson unpacks the rules from the text on how he should respond when his premise is denied, his evidence is invalidated, or a counter-argument is presented. Understanding these roles is essential for structuring a valid disputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the attack type
Determine whether the questioner has denied a premise (munaqada), invalidated the evidence (taqdiyya), or presented a counter-argument (mu'arada).
2
Match the response to munaqada
If the premise is denied, the mu'allil must prove it with evidence, indicate it from prior consensus, or invalidate the questioner's chain if equivalent. He may also prove his claim with a different evidence.
Mere denial by the questioner is useless; the mu'allil must actively respond.
3
Match the response to taqdiyya
If the evidence is invalidated, the mu'allil must either deny the invalidating point or bring another evidence for his claim.
He cannot simply repeat the same evidence.
4
Match the response to mu'arada
If a counter-argument is presented, the mu'allil must engage with that counter-argument. At this point, his role temporarily shifts to that of a questioner, and the questioner becomes the mu'allil.
This is a role reversal until the counter-argument is resolved.
5
Recognize the transmitter exception
If the mu'allil is merely transmitting from an authority, he is not defending a claim; he only needs to verify the transmission is correct.
The questioner cannot demand proof of the claim itself from a transmitter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا وَظِيفَةُ الْمُعَلِّلِ ، أَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُنَاقَضَةِ، فَإِثْبَاتُ الْمُقَدِّمَةِ الْمَمْنُوعَةِ بِالدَّلِيلِ أَوْ بِالتَّنْبِيهِ، أَوْ إِبْطَالُ سَنَدِهِ إِنْ كَانَ مُسَاوِيًا لَهُ؛ إِذْ مَنْعُهُ مُجَرَّدًا غَيْرُ مُفِيدٍ، أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلِ آخَرَ . وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ التَّقْضِ ، فَنَفْيْ شَاهِدِهِ بِالْمَنْعِ، أَوْ إِثْبَاتُ مُدَّعَاهُ بِدَلِيلٍ آخَرَ . وَأَمَّا عِنْدَ الْمُعَارَضَةِ، فَالتَّعَرُّضُ لِدَلِيل الْمُعَارِض؛ إِذْ يَصِيرُ الْمُعَلِّلُ حِينَئِذٍ كَالسَّائِلِ، وَبِالْعَكْسِ. ثُمَّ إِنَّ مَنْ يَكُونُ بِصَدَدِ التَّعْلِيلِ قَدْ لَا يَكُونُ مُدَّعِيًا ، بَلْ يَكُونُ نَاقِلًا عَنِ الْغَيْرِ، فَلَا يَتَوَجَّهُ عَلَيْهِ الْمَنْعُ ، بَلْ يُطْلَبُ مِنْهُ تَصْحِيحُ النَّقْلِ فَقَطْ . هَذَا الَّذِي ذَكَرْنَاهُ طَرِيقُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ، وَأَمَّا مَالُهَا فَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْلُو، إِمَّا أَنْ يَعْجِزَ الْمُعِلِّلُ عَنْ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى مُدَّعَاهُ وَيَسْكُتَ، فَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِفْحَامُ. أَوْ يَعْجِزَ السَّائِلُ عَنِ التَّعَرُّض لَهُ بِأَنْ يَنتَهِيَ دَلِيلُ الْمُعَلِّلِ إِلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ ضَرُورِيَّةِ الْقَبُولِ أَوْ مُسَلَّمَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِلْزَامُ . فَحِينَئِذٍ تَنتَهِي الْمُنَاظَرَةُ، إِذْ لَا قُدْرَةَ لَهُمَا عَلَى إِقَامَةِ وَظَائِفِهِمَا لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ .
As for the function of the one who gives the reason (al-mu'allil): in the case of contradiction (al-munaqada), it is to prove the denied premise by evidence or by indication, or to invalidate his chain [of evidence] if it is equivalent to his; for mere denial is useless, or to prove his claim by another evidence. And in the case of invalidation (al-taqdiyya), it is to negate his evidence by denial, or to prove his claim by another evidence. And in the case of opposition (al-mu'arada), it is to engage with the evidence of the opponent; for then the mu'allil becomes like the questioner, and vice versa. Then, one who is engaged in giving a reason may not be a claimant, but rather a transmitter from others, so denial is not directed at him; rather, he is only required to verify the transmission. This that we have mentioned is the method of disputation. As for its outcome: it is that either the mu'allil is unable to produce evidence for his claim and remains silent—that is silencing (ifham). Or the questioner is unable to engage with it because the mu'allil's evidence reaches a necessarily acceptable premise or an admitted one—that is compelling (ilzam). Then the disputation ends, since neither of them has the ability to perform their functions indefinitely.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage defines the duties of the mu'allil (the party providing the rationale or evidence) in a debate (munazara) according to the science of adab al-bahth. The author first details the mu'allil's function at three distinct stages: when his premise is contradicted (munaqada), when his evidence is invalidated (taqdiyya), and when an opposing argument is presented (mu'arada). In each case, he must either prove his claim with new evidence or nullify the opponent's proof. Notably, if the mu'allil is merely transmitting from someone else, he is not subject to denial but must only verify the transmission. Next, the passage describes the two possible outcomes of debate: if the mu'allil cannot provide evidence and falls silent, that is ifham (silencing); if the questioner (sa'il) is unable to refute the mu'allil's argument because it rests on a self-evident or admitted premise, that is ilzam (compelling). In either case, the debate terminates because neither party can continue their roles indefinitely. This passage clarifies the procedural logic of classical Islamic disputation, emphasizing the burden of proof and the end conditions of debate.

الْمُعَلِّل
الْمُنَاقَضَة
التَّقْض
الْمُعَارَضَة

B) Prove the denied premise by evidence or indication, or invalidate the questioner's chain if equivalent, or prove his claim with another evidence.
C) Immediately counter-attack the questioner's position.
D) Request a new topic of discussion.

Key Takeaway
The mu'allil's duties vary with the type of objection: he must match his response to the stage of debate, and transmitters only need to verify their source.

The Two Endings of Debate: Ifham and Ilzam
Author describes the mu'allil's functions at each stage and the two possible endings of debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two outcomes of debate (ifham and ilzam) and explain why each terminates the discussion.

Core Idea
A debate ends when one party can no longer perform their function: either the mu'allil is silent (ifham) or the questioner is compelled (ilzam).

Every structured debate must come to an end. This lesson explains the two possible conclusions in Islamic disputation: ifham, where the mu'allil cannot support his claim and falls silent, and ilzam, where the questioner cannot refute because the argument rests on a self-evident or admitted premise. Understanding these outcomes is crucial for knowing when a debate is truly concluded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the losing party
Determine which party becomes unable to continue: if the mu'allil cannot produce evidence, it's ifham; if the questioner cannot engage with the evidence, it's ilzam.
2
Analyze ifham
Ifham occurs when the mu'allil fails to bring evidence for his claim and falls silent. The questioner thus silences him.
This is not a refutation of the claim itself but a failure to defend it.
3
Analyze ilzam
Ilzam occurs when the mu'allil's evidence reaches a premise that is necessarily acceptable (daruriyya) or admitted by the questioner (musallama). The questioner cannot object, so the mu'allil compels acceptance.
The premise is so evident that disputing it would be irrational.
4
Confirm the debate ends
In both cases, the debate terminates because neither party can continue fulfilling their roles indefinitely. The text explicitly states: 'Then the disputation ends.'
The end is final; no further rounds are possible.

Source Text

The Two Endings of a Debate
Author defines the two possible conclusions of a debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-ifḥām and al-ilzām in a debate and identify which outcome occurs based on the parties' abilities.

Core Idea
The debate ends when one party is incapacitated: silencing (ifḥām) or binding (ilzām).

This passage from al-Akhḍarī's handbook defines the two possible conclusions of a debate. Understanding these endings is crucial for knowing when a debate has been resolved and who has 'won' according to the rules of adab al-bahth. In Islamic scholarly discourse, debates are not endless; they come to a definite close when one side can no longer continue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two outcomes
Find the two terms introduced: al-ifḥām and al-ilzām. They are the only possible endings of a debate mentioned in the passage.
2
Pinpoint the incapacitated party
Al-ifḥām occurs when the defender (muʿallil) is unable to provide evidence and falls silent. Al-ilzām occurs when the questioner (sāʾil) cannot object because the defender's proof rests on a necessary or conceded premise.
The party who fails is different in each case.
3
Identify the cause of incapacitation
For ifḥām: lack of evidence from the defender. For ilzām: the questioner faces a premise that is undeniably true or already agreed upon, so they have no valid objection.
4
Apply to a concrete scenario
Imagine a debate on whether nabidh is forbidden. If the defender says 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'Nabidh is intoxicating', the questioner cannot challenge these premises—this is ilzām. If the defender cannot produce any evidence for his claim, he is silenced—this is ifḥām.
This step internalises the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا الَّذِي ذَكَرْنَاهُ طَرِيقُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ، وَأَمَّا مَالُهَا فَهُوَ أَنَّهُ لَا يَخْلُو، إِمَّا أَنْ يَعْجِزَ الْمُعِلِّلُ عَنْ إِقَامَةِ الدَّلِيلِ عَلَى مُدَّعَاهُ وَيَسْكُتَ، فَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِفْحَامُ. أَوْ يَعْجِزَ السَّائِلُ عَنِ التَّعَرُّض لَهُ بِأَنْ يَنتَهِيَ دَلِيلُ الْمُعَلِّلِ إِلَى مُقَدِّمَةٍ ضَرُورِيَّةِ الْقَبُولِ أَوْ مُسَلَّمَةٍ، وَذَلِكَ هُوَ الْإِلْزَامُ . فَحِينَئِذٍ تَنتَهِي الْمُنَاظَرَةُ، إِذْ لَا قُدْرَةَ لَهُمَا عَلَى إِقَامَةِ وَظَائِفِهِمَا لَا إِلَى نِهَايَةٍ .
This which we have mentioned is the method of debate. As for its outcome, it is that it is either: the defender (muʿallil) becomes incapable of establishing evidence for his claim and remains silent—that is al-ifḥām; or the questioner becomes incapable of objecting to him, such that the defender's evidence ends at a premise that is necessarily acceptable or conceded—that is al-ilzām. Then at that point the debate ends, since neither of them has the ability to continue performing their functions indefinitely.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage defines two possible outcomes of a debate: if the defender (the one providing a cause or proof) fails to bring evidence for his claim and remains silent, that is إفحام (silencing). If the questioner (the one asking) fails to object because the defender's reasoning ends at a premise that is either inherently necessary or conceded by both parties, that is إلزام (binding). The debate then concludes because neither party can continue. For example, in a fiqh debate about the prohibition of nabidh, if the defender establishes that every intoxicant is forbidden and that nabidh is intoxicating, the questioner cannot object to these premises—this is إلزام. If the defender cannot produce evidence, he is إفحام.

Key Terms
الإفحام
الإلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the defender cannot provide any evidence for his claim.
B) When the defender's evidence ends at a premise that the questioner cannot challenge because it is necessary or conceded.
C) When the questioner remains silent due to ignorance.
D) When both parties agree on the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The debate ends when one party can no longer fulfill their role: the defender by silence (ifḥām) or the questioner by inability to object (ilzām).

?

The Manners of a Debater: A Code of Conduct
Author enumerates the proper conduct for a debater.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize the etiquette rules for a debater into speech-related, process-related, and behavioral categories, and apply them to a hypothetical debate scenario.

Core Idea
Good debate requires disciplined speech, focused process, and respectful conduct.

In this passage, the author distills the essential manners every debater must observe. The rules cover three areas: what to say and how to say it, how to handle the flow of discussion, and how to behave toward the opponent. Mastering these guidelines transforms debate from a chaotic quarrel into a disciplined search for truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three domains of etiquette
Read the passage and notice that the prohibitions and permissions fall into three groups: speech (words), process (how to engage), and behavior (demeanor toward opponent).
2
Extract the speech-related rules
List what the author prohibits regarding speech: excessive brevity (ījāz), verbosity (iṭnāb), strange words, and ambiguous expressions. Note the one exception: asking for clarification (istifsār) is allowed.
Compare with rhetorical use of ījāz; here it is a flaw.
3
Extract the process-related rules
Find the two process guidelines: do not enter discussion before fully understanding the opponent (avoid premature response), but repetition for clarity is fine. Also, avoid irrelevant topics.
4
Extract the behavioral rules
Note the prohibitions on laughing, raising the voice, and similar unbecoming conduct. Also: do not debate with people of dignity and respect in a challenging manner, and never consider the opponent insignificant.
These preserve the dignity of the exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا آدَابُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ، فَهِيَ أَنَّهُ يَنْبَغِي لِلْمُنَاظِرِ أَنْ يَحْتَرِزَ عَنِ الْإِيجَازِ، وَعَنِ الْإِطْنَابِ، وَعَنِ اسْتِعْمَالِ الْأَلْفَاظِ الْغَرِيبَةِ، وَعَنِ اللَّفْظِ الْمُجْمَلِ، وَلَا بَأْسَ بِالاسْتِفْسَارِ. وَعَنِ الدَّخْلِ قَبْلَ الْفَهْمِ وَلَا بَأْسَ بِالْإِعَادَةِ، وَعَنِ التَّعَرُّضِ لِمَا لَا دَخَلَ لَهُ فِي الْمَقْصُودِ، وَعَنِ الضَّحِكِ وَرَفْعِ الصَّوْتِ وَأَمْثَالِهِمَا، وَعَنِ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ مَعَ أَهْلِ الْمَهَابَةِ وَالِاحْتِرَامِ، وَأَلَّا يَحْسِبَ الْخَصْمَ حَقِيرًا. هَذَا غَايَةُ مَا يُرَادُ فِي هَذَا الْبَابِ، وَمِنَ اللَّهِ التَّوْفِيقُ وَإِلْهَامُ الصَّوَابِ.
As for the etiquette of debate: the debater should avoid brevity (ījāz), verbosity (iṭnāb), the use of strange words, and ambiguous expressions; but there is no harm in asking for clarification. And he should avoid entering into discussion before understanding, but there is no harm in repetition. And he should avoid addressing what is irrelevant to the purpose, and avoid laughing, raising the voice, and the like. And he should avoid debating with people of dignity and respect, and he should not consider the opponent as insignificant. This is the utmost desired in this chapter. And from Allah is success and inspiration of correctness.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage lists the manners a debater must observe. The author begins with speech-related manners: avoid excessive brevity (which causes confusion) and excessive verbosity (which bores or distracts). Avoid unusual words and ambiguous expressions; however, asking for clarification (istifsār) is permissible. Next, he addresses the process: do not enter the debate before fully understanding the opponent's point; repetition for clarity is fine. Stay focused on the issue; avoid irrelevant topics. Then behavioral guidelines: avoid laughter, loud voice, and similar unbecoming conduct. Finally, personal respect: do not debate with those who are due deference (like elders or scholars) in a challenging manner, and never consider the opponent beneath you. The author concludes that this is the complete set of etiquette for this subject, asking Allah for success.

Defining Munazara: The Purpose of Dialectic
Author opens the treatise with praise, then defines munazara as mutual investigation for truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of the definition of munazara and distinguish it from mere disputation.

Core Idea
Munazara is mutual investigation for truth, not victory.

In this opening passage, the author lays the foundation for the entire science of debate by defining its core activity: munazara. The definition is concise but packed with meaning, emphasizing that it is a joint intellectual effort aimed at revealing truth. Understanding this definition is crucial because it frames everything that follows in the treatise, from the roles of the participants to the rules of engagement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic text. It is 'al-munāẓara' (المناظرة).
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'naẓar bi al-baṣīra' (نظر بالبصيرة), i.e., intellectual investigation using inner insight. The differentia is 'min al-jānibayn' (من الجانبين) — involving two sides, and the purpose 'iẓhāran li al-ṣawāb' (إظهارًا للصواب) — to manifest truth.
3
Analyze the key terms
Note that 'naẓar bi al-baṣīra' distinguishes munazara from mere sensory observation or random argument. It is a deliberate, reasoned investigation.
4
Apply the definition to a simple case
Consider two scholars debating whether a certain analogy (qiyas) is valid. They investigate together, each presenting evidence, aiming to uncover the correct ruling. This is munazara.

بسمِ اللهِ الرَّحْمنِ الرَّحِيمِ. أَحْمَدُكَ اللَّهُمَّ يَا مُجِيبَ كُلِّ سَائِلِ، وَأَصَلِّي عَلَى نَبِيِّكَ الْمَبْعُوثِ بِأَقْوَى الدَّلَائِلِ، وَعَلَى آلِهِ وَأَصْحَابِهِ الْمُتَوَسِّلِينَ بِأَعْظَمِ الْوَسَائِلِ، مَا جَرَى الْبَحْثُ بَيْنَ الْمُجِيبِ وَالسَّائِلِ. وَبَعْدُ، فَهَذِهِ رِسَالَةٌ لَخَصْتُهَا فِي عِلْمِ الْآدَابِ، مُجْتَنِبًا عَنْ طَرَفَيِ الاقْتِصَادِ: الْإِخْلَالِ وَالْإِطْنَابِ، وَاللهَ أَسْأَلُ أَنْ يَنْفَعَ بِهَا مَعَاشِرَ الطُّلابِ، وَمَا تَوْفِيقِي إِلَّا بِاللهِ عَلَيْهِ تَوَكَّلْتُ، وَإِلَيْهِ الْمَآبُ. اعْلَمْ أَنَّ الْمُنَاظَرَةَ: هِيَ النَّظَرُ بِالْبَصِيرَةِ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْنِ فِي النِّسْبَةِ بَيْنَ الشَّيْتَيْنِ؛ إِظْهَارًا لِلصَّوَابِ. وَلِكُلِّ مِنَ الْجَانِبَيْن وَظَائِفُ، وَلِلْمُنَاظَرَةِ آدَابٌ.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. I praise You, O Allah, O Answerer of every asker, and I send blessings upon Your Prophet sent with the strongest proofs, and upon his family and companions who seek intermediation through the greatest means, as long as discussion flows between the responder and the asker. To proceed: This is a treatise I have summarized concerning the science of etiquette, avoiding both extremes: deficiency and verbosity. And I ask Allah to benefit thereby the groups of students. And my success is only from Allah; upon Him I rely, and to Him is the return. Know that al-munāẓara is: the gaze with insight from both sides concerning the relation between two things, for the manifestation of correctness. And for each of the two sides there are functions, and for munāẓara there are etiquettes.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the treatise. It begins with the basmala, followed by praise of Allah and blessings on the Prophet, then a statement of purpose: the author summarizes the science of etiquette (ʿilm al-ādāb) avoiding two extremes—deficiency (ikhlāl) and verbosity (iṭnāb). He prays for benefit. Then he defines munāẓara: it is 'the gaze with insight (naẓar bi al-baṣīra) from both sides concerning the relation between two things, for the manifestation of correctness'. The phrase 'gaze with insight' indicates that it is not mere staring but intellectual investigation (baṣīra = inner sight). 'From both sides' (min al-jānibayn) means two parties are involved, each looking at the relationship between two entities (shay'ayn). The purpose is to reveal truth (iẓhāran li al-ṣawāb). The author then states that each side has functions (waẓāʾif) and that munāẓara has etiquettes (ādāb), which will be explained. This sets up the treatise. The definition is concise and foundational: it distinguishes munāẓara from mere disputation or argumentation by emphasizing mutual investigation aimed at truth.

The Purpose of Munazara: Truth, Not Victory
Author defines munazara as joint truth-seeking inquiry

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining features of munazara as a joint truth-seeking inquiry and distinguish it from mere argumentation.

Core Idea
Munazara is not a contest but a collaborative search for truth through rational insight.

This passage opens the treatise by defining the very subject of study: munazara. The definition is precise and intentional, framing debate as a cooperative intellectual effort rather than a competitive quarrel. Understanding this foundational definition is crucial, as the entire treatise builds upon it to elaborate the functions of each participant and the etiquette required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the word 'المناظرة' in the Arabic. This is the definiendum. The author begins 'اعلم أن المناظرة: هي...' which signals a definition is coming.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'النظر بالبصيرة' – insightful examination, not any ordinary looking or arguing. The differentia includes two parts: (1) it is 'من الجانبين' (from both sides), and (2) it concerns 'النسبة بين الشيئين' (the relation between two things). These distinguish munazara from solitary reflection or one-sided argument.
A complete hadd must include both genus and differentia. Here the differentia captures both the collaborative aspect and the object of investigation.
3
Understand the purpose clause
The definition ends with 'إظهارا للصواب' – to manifest the truth. This is the final cause of munazara. It tells us that the goal is not to win but to bring truth to light. Any exchange that lacks this purpose is not true munazara.
This clause is essential for distinguishing munazara from sophistry or polemic.
4
Note the components that follow
The author then mentions that each side has functions (وظائف) and the activity has etiquette (آداب). These are elaborations that will be detailed later. Recognizing this structure helps you anticipate the rest of the treatise.

Commentary
This passage defines the central subject of the treatise: munazara. It is not a mere argument but a joint intellectual investigation (النظر بالبصيرة) from both parties into the relationship between two propositions or things, aiming to bring out the truth (إظهارا للصواب). The author then notes that each participant has specific functions, and the process itself has a set of etiquette (آداب) that must be observed. This definition sets the stage for the rest of the treatise, which will elaborate on these functions and etiquette.

Three Valid Objections in Debate: Munāqaḍa, Naqḍ, Mu‘āraḍa
Author enumerates the three types of objection the questioner may raise, with conditions for validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three valid types of objection (munāqaḍa, naqḍ, mu‘āraḍa) in an Islamic dialectical exchange based on their target and conditions.

Core Idea
Not every denial is allowed; each objection targets a specific layer of the proof.

In Islamic dialectics (munāẓara), the questioner is not free to object arbitrarily. This passage from the Risālat Ādāb al-Baḥth lays out exactly which objections are permissible and which are not. You will learn to identify the three accepted forms—munāqaḍa, naqḍ, and mu‘āraḍa—by their target and the conditions that make them valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three targets of objection
The passage states that the questioner can object to one of three things: the premise (muqaddima) of the proof, the proof itself (dalīl), or the conclusion (madlūl). First, locate these three targets in the Arabic text.
2
Match each target to its objection type
For each target, the author assigns a specific name: objection to the premise is munāqaḍa, objection to the proof is naqḍ, and objection to the conclusion is mu‘āraḍa. Confirm that the Arabic terms correspond correctly.
3
Check the conditions for each type
The passage specifies what is and is not allowed for each objection. For munāqaḍa: mere denial or denial with a chain (sanad) is allowed, but denial with a counter-proof (dalīl) is forbidden (ghaṣb). For naqḍ: denial with a witness (shāhid) is allowed; denial without a witness is stubbornness (mukābara). For mu‘āraḍa: denial with a proof (dalīl) is allowed; denial without a proof is stubbornness.
4
Understand the forbidden forms
The passage emphasizes two forbidden acts: ghaṣb (usurpation) and mukābara (stubbornness). Ghaṣb occurs when the questioner denies a premise by bringing a counter-proof, which confuses the debate. Mukābara is any denial without evidence, unanimously rejected.
The exception for ghaṣb is if the questioner first establishes his own proof for the denied premise after it is challenged.
5
Apply to a practice scenario
Given a hypothetical debate exchange, identify which type of objection is being used and whether it follows the rules. For example: The answerer says 'The world is temporally originated.' The questioner says 'No, because the world is eternal.' Is this valid? (Answer: It attacks the premise with a counter-proof, so it is ghaṣb and not allowed.)

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما وظيفة السائل فثلاثة: المناقضة، والنقض، والمعارضة؛ لأنه إما أن يمنع مقدمة الدليل، أو الدليل نفسه، أو المدلول. فإن كان الأول: فإن منع مجردا، أو بالسند فهو المناقضة، ومنها نوع يسمى بالحل، وهو تعيين موضع الغلط. وأما منعه بالدليل فهو غصب غير مسموع عند المحققين؛ لاستلزامه الخبط. نعم قد يتوجه ذلك بعد إقامة الدليل على تلك المقدمة الممنوعة. وإن كان الثاني: فإن منع بالشاهد فهو النقض، وأما منعه بلا شاهد فهو مكابرة غير مسموعة اتفاقا. وإن كان الثالث: فإن منع بالدليل فهو المعارضة، وأما منعه بلا دليل فهو مكابرة غير مسموعة أيضا اتفاقا.
As for the function of the questioner, it is threefold: munāqaḍa (contradiction), naqḍ (refutation by counterexample), and mu‘āraḍa (counter-argument). Because he either prevents (denies) the premise of the proof, or the proof itself, or the conclusion (the thing proven). If it is the first: then if he prevents it merely (without evidence) or with a chain of transmission, it is munāqaḍa. Among its types is what is called al-ḥall (the resolution), which is pinpointing the location of the error. As for preventing it with a proof, it is usurpation (ghaṣb) not listened to according to the verifiers, because it necessitates confusion. Yes, that may be directed after establishing the proof for that prevented premise. If it is the second: then if he prevents it with a witness (evidence), it is naqḍ. As for preventing it without a witness, it is stubbornness (mukābara) not listened to unanimously. If it is the third: then if he prevents it with a proof, it is mu‘āraḍa. As for preventing it without a proof, it is also stubbornness not listened to unanimously.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage details the three valid functions of the questioner in a debate. The questioner can attack one of three elements: the premise of the proof (مقدمة الدليل), the proof itself (الدليل نفسه), or the conclusion (المدلول). Each attack is given a specific name and conditions. 1) If he attacks the premise, and does so without bringing a counter-proof (just denying it or giving a chain of transmission), it is called munāqaḍa. A subtype is al-ḥall, which is identifying the exact error. However, if he denies the premise by bringing a counter-proof (i.e., proving the opposite), that is not accepted according to the verifiers because it leads to confusion (خبط) – it is considered ghaṣb (usurpation of the role of the answerer). The exception is if he first establishes his own proof for the opposite premise. 2) If he attacks the proof itself, by bringing a counter-example (شاهد), it is called naqḍ. Without a witness, it is mere stubbornness (مكابرة) and unanimously not accepted. 3) If he attacks the conclusion by bringing a counter-proof, it is mu‘āraḍa. Without a proof, it is also stubbornness. This classification shows the strict rules of debate in Islamic dialectics.

Key Terms
المناقضة
النقض
المعارضة
الحل
الغصب
المكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Islamic dialectics, the questioner has three valid objection types, each targeting a specific element of the proof and requiring specific conditions to be acceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
